M. K. F. v. Miramontes


SC S058847


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


352 Ore. 401; 287 P.3d 1045; 2012 Ore. LEXIS 669


March 6, 2012, Argued and Submitted 
September 20, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 0510408; CA A138024. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Benton County Circuit Court, David B. Connell, Judge. 236 Ore. App. 381, 236 P.3d 782 (2010).


Foster v. Miramontes, 236 Ore. App. 381, 236 P.3d 782, 2010 Ore. App. LEXIS 889 (2010)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed in part and reversed in part. The case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff filed a petition under Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.866, alleging knowing and repeated unwanted sexual contact by defendant, and seeking both a stalking protective order and a judgment for compensatory money damages. The trial court conducted the trial without a jury and found for plaintiff, including an award of $42,347 in compensatory damages. The court of appeals affirmed, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court of Oregon held that held that in an action under Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.866 in which a plaintiff sought both a stalking protective order and a judgment for compensatory money damages, the parties were entitled to a jury trial on the claim for money damages. Or. Const. art. I, § 17, and Or. Const. art. VII (Amended) § 3, did not guarantee a right to jury trial for claims or request for relief that, standing alone, were equitable in nature and would have been tried to a court without a jury at common law. By the same token, those provisions did guarantee a right to jury trial on claims or requests that were properly categorized as "civil" or "at law," in the absence of a showing that the nature of a claim or request for relief was such that it would have been tried to a court without a jury. Plaintiff's claim seeking monetary damage for injury inflicted was properly categorized as civil or at law, even if it did not have a precise historical analog. The Court of Appeals erred in reaching a contrary conclusion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed decision of the court of appeals with regard to the right to jury trial on the claim for compensatory damages and reversed the circuit court's judgment as to compensatory damages. The court otherwise affirmed the judgment. The case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

State v. McBride


SC S059650


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


352 Ore. 159; 281 P.3d 605; 2012 Ore. LEXIS 434


March 6, 2012, Argued and Submitted 
June 28, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 07C50799; CA A139020. On review from the Court of Appeals.*


* Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Albin W. Norblad III, Judge. 242 Ore. App. 594, 256 P3d 174 (2011).


State v. McBride, 242 Ore. App. 594, 256 P.3d 174, 2011 Ore. App. LEXIS 665 (2011)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed in part and affirmed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of violating Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 475.856, 475.906, and 163.575(1)(b). The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed his convictions. Defendant appealed only his child-endangerment convictions under § 163.575(1)(b)


OVERVIEW: Defendant renewed his argument that he had insufficient authority over either the teenagers or the home to have permitted the teenagers to enter or remain there. In his view, a person may not permit what that person lacks the authority to prohibit. The state argued for a broader meaning of permit. The illegal conduct was the act of permitting a minor to enter or remain where illegal drug activity was occurring. Section 163.575(1)(b) did not require a person to have legal authority over a minor or over the place where drug activity was occurring. In the present case, the state had to demonstrate that defendant engaged in some affirmative conduct authorizing or otherwise making it possible for the minors to enter or remain in the home. The evidence missed that mark. Its emphasis on his role in maintaining an illegal drug operation in a place where he knew that minors would remain therefore did not advance its case. Its emphasis on his role in maintaining an illegal drug operation in a place where he knew that minors would remain did not advance its case in the absence of actual authority.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals was reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed in part and affirmed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court. The circuit court erred in denying defendant's motion for judgment of acquittal on the child-endangerment charges.

State v. Miskell


SC S059326


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


351 Ore. 680; 277 P.3d 522; 2012 Ore. LEXIS 269


November 10, 2011, Argued and Submitted 
April 26, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 08C46146; CA A140390 (Control). CC 08C46147; CA A140391. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Albin W. Norblad, III, Judge. 239 Ore App 629, 246 P3d 755 (2010).


State v. Miskell, 239 Ore. App. 629, 246 P.3d 755, 2010 Ore. App. LEXIS 1705 (2010)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted of aggravated first-degree theft, Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.057, and second-degree burglary, Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.215. The Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon) affirmed. Defendants petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court erred by denying defendants' motion to suppress the recording and transcript of a conversation between defendants and an accomplice under Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.736(1) because the circumstances at the time that the officer decided to go forward with the monitoring operation were not exigent, and therefore the officer should have obtained a court order allowing the conversation to be recorded. The officer stated that he did not obtain a court order because he preferred to wind up the investigation into the burglary that night or as soon as possible, and the evidence showed that a court order could have been obtained that night had the officer chosen to do so. The court held that the error was not harmless because the recorded conversation was the only evidence that corroborated the accomplice's assertion that defendants were the primary actors in the burglary and therefore went to a key element of the State's case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

White v. Public Emples. Ret. Bd.


SC S059213


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


351 Ore. 426; 268 P.3d 600; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 1025


May 3, 2011, Argued and Submitted 
December 30, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 040404118, 041111848; CA A142773. On certified appeal from the Court of Appeals on appeal from a judgment of the Circuit Court for Multnomah County, Henry J. Kantor, Judge.
White v. Public Emples. Ret. Bd., 349 Ore. 656, 249 P.3d 543, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 161 (2011)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, one retired member and two active members of Oregon's Public Employees Retirement System (PERS), challenged a summary judgment from the Circuit Court for Multnomah County (Oregon), which ruled that a settlement between defendant Public Employees Retirement Board (PERB) and intervenor public employers did not breach PERB's fiduciary duties to the members. The Court of Appeals certified the appeal to the Oregon Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The public employers brought suit challenging PERB orders that increased their contribution rates and that credited the regular accounts of PERS Tier One members at a higher rate. After an adverse circuit court decision and several legislative changes unfavorable to it, PERB agreed to settle. The court held that PERB, established as a trustee and directed to ensure the long-term stability and viability of the retirement system pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 238.601, 238.660(1), did not breach its fiduciary duties to the members under former Or. Rev. Stat. § 128.009 and the common law as to most aspects of the settlement. PERB reasonably exercised its discretion under Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.661 when it allocated earnings above the assumed earnings rate to accounts other than active member accounts, entered into the settlement based on an assessment that it was unlikely to prevail on appeal, dismissed its appeal, and paid the public employers' attorney fees. New rates, revised crediting orders, and certain implementing acts were proper. Disputed fact issues existed as to the reasonableness of a transfer from the contingency reserve account established by Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.670(1).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portion of the summary judgment regarding the transfer from the contingency reserve, remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings as to that issue, and otherwise affirmed.

Williams v. RJ Reynolds Tobacco Co.


SC S059014 (Control), SC S059248


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


351 Ore. 368; 271 P.3d 103; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 881; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P18,741


September 19, 2011, Argued and Submitted 
December 2, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 970503957, CC 970604457. On appeal certified from the Court of Appeals on appeal from a judgment of the Multnomah County Circuit Court. Janice R. Wilson, Judge.
State v. Am. Tobacco Co., 349 Ore. 665, 249 P.3d 1283, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 214 (2011)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the circuit court in Williams v. Philip Morris (CC970503957) is reversed, and the case is remanded for entry of a judgment consistent with this opinion. The post-judgment order in State v. American Tobacco Co. (CC 970604457) is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury awarded an estate $79.5 million in punitive damages for defendant tobacco company's fraud and negligence leading to a smoker's lung cancer death. The manufacturer refused to pay plaintiff, the State of Oregon, the 60 percent of punitive damages due under Or. Rev. Stat. § 31.735. The trial court ruled that the state had released its claim, and the Court of Appeals, Oregon, certified the appeal to the Oregon Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The Oregon Supreme Court held that the state's right to a share of punitive damages under Oregon's split recovery statute was not a "released claim," as that term was defined in the master settlement agreement in multi-state tobacco litigation. Therefore, the state did not release its right to pursue payment of its statutory interest in 60 percent of the punitive damages award when it settled that other action. The state released only those claims that were directly or indirectly based on the tobacco company's described tobacco-related conduct, but the state's interest in the 60 percent share arose by operation of law. A claim to collect on the judgment would be to enforce the statute, not to recover damages for tobacco-related conduct. The fact that there was a but-for causal connection between the current case and the state's entitlement to part of the punitive damages award did not change the legal nature of the state's interest in its share of the award. The state's interest in the punitive damages award arose out of a statute that was indifferent to the factual basis of the underlying litigation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case for entry of a judgment. The court also reversed a post-judgment order in the multi-state tobacco litigation.

Arken v. City of Portland


SC S058881 (Control), SC S058882


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


351 Ore. 113; 263 P.3d 975; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 718


January 6, 2011, Argued and Submitted 
October 6, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at Goodson v. Public Emples. Ret. Sys., 351 Ore. 173, 264 P.3d 148, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 715 (2011)
Adhered to, On reconsideration by Robinson v. Public Emples. Ret. Bd., 351 Ore. 404, 268 P.3d 567, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 1024 (Or., Dec. 30, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 060100536; CC 060504584. On certified appeals from the Court of Appeals on appeals from judgments of the Circuit Court for Multnomah County, Henry J. Kantor, Judge.
Robinson v. Public Emples. Ret. Bd., 247 P.3d 761, 2010 Ore. LEXIS 826 (Or., 2010)
Arken v. City of Portland, 247 P.3d 761, 2010 Ore. LEXIS 827 (Or., 2010)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the circuit court in Arken, et al. v. City of Portland, et al., Case No. 0601-00536, is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court in Robinson, et al. v. Public Employees Retirement Board, Case No. 0605-04584, is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two consolidated cases, appellant the Public Employees Retirement Board (PERB) challenged summary judgment granted by the Circuit Court for Multnomah County, Oregon, in appellee retirees' suit to prevent the recovery of overpayments of benefits to the retirees.


OVERVIEW: After determining that a certain class of retirees had received overpayments of the benefits to which they were entitled, the PERB established a general method for the recover of overpayments made to those retirees. The retirees filed suit challenging the PERB's order adopting repayment methods, and the circuit court granted summary judgment in their favor. On review, the court held that the circuit court erred in granting summary judgment in favor of the retirees and denying the PERB's cross-motion for summary judgment because the PERB correctly applied Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.715 to recoup the overpayments made to the retirees, as Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.715 provided explicit authority to the PERB to recover overpayments. Further, categorically treating the erroneous overpayments to all of the retirees at issue as an administrative expense violated well-established trust fund principles that are embedded in Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.660 because that treatment favors the retirees over other beneficiaries of the Public Employees Retirement System (PERS).


OUTCOME: In one of the two cases, summary judgment granted in favor of the PERB was affirmed. In the second case, summary judgment granted in favor of the retirees was reversed, and the case was remanded with instructions to enter judgment in favor of the PERB.

Lasley v. Combined Transp., Inc.


SC S058762


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


351 Ore. 1; 261 P.3d 1215; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 714


May 2, 2011, Argued and Submitted 
September 22, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 0608-08260; CA A137222. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, John Wittmayer, Judge. 234 Ore. App. 11, 227 P.3d 1200, adh'd to on recons, 236 Ore. App. 1, 237 P.3d 859 (2010).


Lasley v. Combined Transp., Inc., 234 Ore. App. 11, 227 P.3d 1200, 2010 Ore. App. LEXIS 193 (2010)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, granted petitioner father's motion in limine to exclude evidence that respondent driver was intoxicated at the time of the collision. Respondent trucking company was found to be 22 percent at fault and the driver 78 percent at fault. The Oregon Court of Appeals reversed, concluding that the trial court erred in excluding the evidence of the driver's intoxication. The father petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the company did not proffer evidence that showed that, because the driver was intoxicated, she inevitably would have killed decedent, even if his pickup had not been stationary. Because the fact of the driver's intoxication would not have made her conduct more significant or the company's conduct less significant in the causation analysis, it was not relevant to that issue. The company did not allege in its cross-claim that it had paid more than its proportional share of liability or sought a money judgment against the driver. It should have included those allegations in its answer as an affirmative defense. The cross-claim that the company proffered fulfilled the function of an affirmative defense, Or. R. Civ. P. 19. The defect in designating the pleading as a cross-claim rather than an affirmative defense did not affect the father's substantial rights. However, the evidence of the driver's intoxication was not relevant on the issues of causation, liability, or damages.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded to the circuit court for a new trial limited to the degree of fault of each defendant expressed as a percentage of the total fault attributable to each defendant.

 

61 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cloutier


SC S059039


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


351 Ore. 68; 261 P.3d 1234; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 711


May 5, 2011, Argued and Submitted 
September 22, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC D085162T; CA A143051. On review from the Court of Appeals.*


* Appeal from Order Denying Reconsideration dated November 3, 2010, David V. Brewer, Chief Judge.


State v. Cloutier, 349 Ore. 663, 249 P.3d 1281, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 213 (2011)



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Appeals is reversed, and the case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for entry of an order dismissing the appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which denied reconsideration after determining that it had jurisdiction under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.050(1)(a) to review the sentence imposed upon defendant after he pleaded no contest to a misdemeanor offense of driving under the influence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant failed to complete a diversion program. The trial court imposed a sentence within the statutory range set forth in Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 161.635(1)(a), 813.010(6)(a). When defendant asked why the trial court had imposed more than the mandatory minimum fine, the trial court explained that the additional amount was for defendant's no contest plea at the time of entering the diversion. Defendant contended on appeal that although the fine imposed was within the statutory range, he had been unconstitutionally penalized for exercising his right to plead no contest. The court held that the asserted due process violation did not cause the sentence to exceed the maximum allowable by law. The reference in § 138.050(1)(a) to a disposition exceeding the maximum allowable by law meant a disposition that exceeded a maximum sentence expressed by means of legislation, not a disposition that violated the Oregon or federal Constitution. Thus, because the amount of defendant's fine was less than the statutory maximum that could have been imposed for the offense, the court of appeals lacked jurisdiction under § 138.050(1)(a), and defendant's proper remedy was to seek post-conviction relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to the court of appeals with directions to dismiss the appeal.

State v. Speedis


SC S058310


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


350 Ore. 424; 256 P.3d 1061; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 558


November 8, 2010, Argued and Submitted 
June 30, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC CF070533; CA A138616. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Umatilla County Circuit Court, Daniel J. Hill, Judge. 233 Ore. App. 297, 225 P.3d 152 (2010).


State v. Speedis, 233 Ore. App. 297, 225 P.3d 152, 2010 Ore. App. LEXIS 30 (2010)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Umatilla County Circuit Court, Oregon, imposed an enhanced sentence based on nonenumerated aggravating factors. The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that a trial court may not rely on nonenumerated aggravating factors to impose an enhanced sentence. He argued that this violated the separation provision of the Oregon Constitution, Or. Const. art. III, § 1. The supreme court found that not only did a presumptive sentence not define the outer boundaries of the trial court's sentencing authority, but the sentencing guidelines expressly authorized trial courts to decide whether nonenumerated aggravating and mitigating factors warranted imposing a greater or a lesser sentence than a presumptive sentence. In imposing a departure sentence based on nonenumerating aggravating factors, a trial court was not acting beyond the bounds of its sentencing authority. The discretion that the sentencing guidelines gave prosecutors to identify and courts to determine nonenumerated aggravating factors was neither standardless nor unfettered. That aspect of the sentencing guidelines was not vague in violation of Or. Const. art. I, §§ 20 and 21. Prior cases provided sufficient guidance to prosecutors in identifying those aggravating factors that would support the imposition of an enhanced sentence under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

OVERVIEW: In 1996, defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment for the aggravated murder of one person and the attempted aggravated murder of another during a 1994 convenience store robbery. In 2004, defendant and another inmate killed a third inmate. Defendant was convicted of aggravated murder and sentenced to death. State v. Stokes


SC S057751


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


350 Ore. 44; 248 P.3d 953; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 167


November 9, 2010, Argued and submitted  
March 10, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Stokes, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 436 (Or., May 5, 2011)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Stokes v. Oregon, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 6998 (U.S., Oct. 3, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC040431760; CA A129130. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Alicia Fuchs, Judge. 229 Ore. App. 97, 211 P3d 381 (2009).


State v. Stokes, 229 Ore. App. 97, 211 P.3d 381, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 923 (2009)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In 2004, the state resubmitted 1996 charges of sexual assault to a second grand jury, along with additional counts of sexual assault of a second victim. A jury of the Multnomah County Circuit Court, Oregon, found defendant guilty of the resulting four counts. The Court of Appeals affirmed the conviction without an opinion. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The Oregon Supreme Court found no error in the resubmission of the 1996 charge to the grand jury. Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 132.430(2), a trial court properly considered whether resubmission would be in the interest of justice, guided by factors such as whether resubmission would be an abuse of the grand jury process or would serve the public interest in bringing a criminal to justice. In the current case, the prosecutor's affidavit established that the state had additional evidence against defendant that had not been available when the first grand jury declined to indict. There was also no due process violation. To demonstrate that preindictment delay violated the Fourteenth Amendment Due Process Clause, a defendant had to show that the delay actually prejudiced the defendant and that the government culpably caused the delay. A court had to weigh the government's reason for the delay against the prejudice to determine whether the delay violated fundamental conceptions of justice, fair play, and decency. In the current case, defendant showed either no actual prejudice, or only the slightest actual prejudice, which was insufficient in light of the lack of government culpability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals and judgment of the circuit court.

State v. Sundberg


SC S058116


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


349 Ore. 608; 247 P.3d 1213; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 110


September 15, 2010, Argued and Submitted 
February 17, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by State v. Sundberg, 268 Ore. App. 577, 2015 Ore. App. LEXIS 67 (2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 05102194; CA A135487. On review from the Court of Appeals.*.


* Appeal from the Linn County Circuit Court, Glen D. Baisinger, Judge. 233 Ore. App. 77, 225 P.3d 89 (2009).


State v. Sundberg, 233 Ore. App. 77, 225 P.3d 89, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 2146 (2009)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals of Oregon that affirmed his convictions of first-degree sexual abuse under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.427 and attempted unlawful sexual penetration under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 161.405(2)(b) and 163.411, arguing that the appellate court erred in finding that he waived his objection to the use of anonymous jurors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with sex crimes following sexual contact with a 10-year-old girl. At trial, the trial court ruled that the names and other personal information of prospective jurors would not be disclosed. A jury was selected and empanelled, and the jury found defendant guilty. Although defendant objected to the use of anonymous jurors at the time of jury selection, the appellate court held that he waived his objection. In deciding an issue of first impression, the court held that, under Or. Rev. Stat. § 10.205(2), trial courts had the authority to require that a jury be seated without disclosing the names, addresses, and employers of prospective jurors to the parties. However, the trial court's authority to empanel an anonymous jury was to be exercised consistent with defendant's constitutional rights. Because Or. Const. art. I, § 11, permitted an anonymous jury only when the trial court found that the circumstances of a particular case justified that practice and took steps to mitigate any prejudice to defendant and because the trial court failed to make the requisite findings or take any steps to prevent prejudice to defendant, the trial court committed reversible error.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, the judgment of the trial court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Hamlin v. Hampton Lumber Mills, Inc.


SC S056700


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


349 Ore. 526; 246 P.3d 1121; 2011 Ore. LEXIS 1; 31 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1646


September 18, 2009, Argued and Submitted 
January 6, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 040302235; CA A130213. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from the Multnomah County Circuit Court, Richard C. Baldwin, Judge. 222 Ore. App. 230, 193 P3d 46 (2008), on recons, 227 Ore. App. 165, 205 P3d 70 (2009).


Hamlin v. Hampton Lumber Mills, Inc., 222 Ore. App. 230, 193 P.3d 46, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1203 (2008)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured employee sued defendant employer for refusing to reinstate him as required by Or. Rev. Stat. § 659A.043. A jury awarded plaintiff lost wages of $6,000 and punitive damages of $175,000. The Court of Appeals, Oregon, reduced the punitive damages award, finding that it was grossly excessive under the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. Plaintiff sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The Court of Appeals primarily relied on the ratio between punitive and compensatory damages in concluding that the award of punitive damages was grossly excessive. On further review, the court held that when a compensatory damages award is small and does not already serve an admonitory function, the ratio between punitive and compensatory damages is of limited assistance in determining whether the amount of a jury's punitive damages award meets or exceeds state goals of deterrence and retribution. Having decided that the punitive damages award in the current case could exceed a single-digit multiplier of the compensatory damages award without violating due process, the court found that punitive damages actually awarded--$175,000--was not, nevertheless, grossly excessive. The compensatory damages were small and the ratio between the punitive and compensatory damages--22:1--was in the low double digits. That ratio was not so high that it made the award grossly excessive. The amount was not so high that the court could say that it exceeded, rather than served, Oregon's interests in deterring and punishing a violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 659A.043.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and reinstated the punitive damages award.

State v. Moore


SC S057820, SC S058145; S058152 (Consolidated for argument and opinion)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


349 Ore. 371; 245 P.3d 101; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 902


May 7, 2010, Argued and Submitted at Portland State University, Portland, Oregon. 
December 16, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC CF050356; CA A134343. CC 020774FE; CA A135115. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Umatilla County Circuit Court, Jan Wyers, Judge. 229 Ore. App. 255, 211 P..3d 344 (2009).


State v. Coen, 231 Ore. App. 280, 220 P.3d 423, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1513 (2009)
State v. Moore, 229 Ore. App. 255, 211 P.3d 344, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 963 (2009)



DISPOSITION: In State v. Douglas Carl Moore, S057820, the decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings. * In State v. Edwin Shane Coen, S058145/S058152, the decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings. **



* Appeal from Umatilla County Circuit Court, Jan Wyers, Judge. 229 Ore. App. 255, 211 P..3d 344 (2009).


** Appeal from Jackson County Circuit Court, Patricia Crain, Judge. 231 Ore. App. 280, 220 P.3d 423 (2009).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed judgments of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) holding, in one case, that it could not consider defendant's trial testimony in determining whether the admission of defendant's unlawfully obtained statement was harmless error, and in the second case that the State could not introduce defendant's prior trial testimony during his retrial.


OVERVIEW: In the two criminal cases, each defendant testified at his trial after the trial court had erroneously admitted incriminating statements that police officers had obtained from each defendant in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 12. The court held that a defendant's trial testimony had to be excluded on retrial or from harmless error review by an appellate court unless the court could determine from the record that a defendant's trial testimony did not refute, explain, or qualify the erroneously admitted pretrial statements. As to the first defendant, the court held that the appellate court corrected reversed defendant's conviction and remanded for a new trial because in his trial testimony, defendant denied that he knew that the functioning rifle was in the vehicle and he did not in any way admit that his pretrial statements were true, and his trial testimony could not in and of itself support a finding of guilt. As to the second defendant, the State did not argue that defendant's decision to testify at trial was untainted by the erroneous admission of his unconstitutionally obtained statements. The court rejected the second defendant's Or. Evid. Code 404(4) was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgments were affirmed and the cases were remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Haugen

Writ of mandamus granted State v. Haugen, 350 Ore. 531, 258 P.3d 526, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 521 (2011)
Writ of mandamus denied State v. Haugen, 2012 Ore. LEXIS 579 (Or., Aug. 16, 2012)
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by Haugen v. Kitzhaber, 353 Ore. 715, 306 P.3d 592, 2013 Ore. LEXIS 417 (2013)
Writ denied by State v. Haugen, 2016 Ore. LEXIS 200 (Or., Mar. 24, 2016)
Related proceeding at Haugen v. Oregon, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 145457 (D. Or., Oct. 20, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 04C46224. On automatic and direct review of the judgment of conviction and sentence of death imposed by the Marion County Circuit Court. Joseph Guimond, Judge.
State v. Hayward, 327 Ore. 397, 963 P.2d 667, 1998 Ore. LEXIS 593 (1998)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction and sentence of death are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a trial under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095(1)(c), (2)(b), the Marion County Circuit Court, Oregon, imposed a judgment of conviction and death sentence for defendant's aggravated murder of another inmate. Direct review was automatic.


OVERVIEW: The court held that there was no Sixth or Fourteenth Amendment error in excusing sua sponte two prospective jurors who lacked proficiency in the English language and who needed an interpreter. The interests articulated by the state--the jurors' ability to follow the proceedings and participate in deliberations and the cost of providing interpreters--were sufficient to justify excluding non-English-speaking persons from the jury pool. The court also held that there was no error in excluding a government witness's statement to defendant--"How do you like me now?"--as he was leaving the stand because a detailed cross-examination of the witness and other evidence adduced at trial showed the witness's dislike of defendant. Finally, the death sentence did not have to be imposed consecutively to the life sentence that defendant was already serving. Because Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.123(3), requiring consecutive sentences for crimes in prison, conflicted with the death penalty statutes, the court relied on the particular legislative intent expressed in Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095(2)(b) that the sentence of death be carried out for an aggravated murder committed in prison.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and sentence of death.

West Linn Corporate Park, L.L.C. v. City of West Linn


SC S056322


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


349 Ore. 58; 240 P.3d 29; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 830



September 23, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
USCA 05-53061, 05-36062. On certified questions from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit; certification order dated July 29, 2008; certification accepted December 10, 2008; argued and submitted September 17, 2009.
W. Linn Corporate Park L.L.C. v. City of W. Linn, 534 F.3d 1091, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 15950 (9th Cir. Or., 2008)



DISPOSITION: The certified questions are answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer filed suit against defendant City of West Linn, Oregon, alleging the city effected a taking of its property when the city required, as a condition of development of that property, that the developer construct off-site public improvements. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit certified three questions to the state supreme court.


OVERVIEW: The developer filed a taking's claim, because the city required it to construct and dedicate to the city public improvements for street and water as a condition of development. The developer did not use available local procedures to seek to challenge the requirement that it construct off-site improvements. The Supreme Court of Oregon held that Oregon law required a landowner to pursue available local administrative remedies, but not to appeal to the Land Use Board of Appeals, as a prerequisite to bringing an action in state court. The developer's allegation that the city required it to construct off-site improvements at a cost that was not roughly proportional to the impact of the development did not allege a taking that would give rise to a claim for just compensation. The city's failure to satisfy the landowner consent requirements of Or. Rev. Stat. § 271.080 did not render void the city's requirement that the developer vacate a portion of the street abutting its property as a condition of development.


OUTCOME: The certified questions were answered.

State v. Partain


SC S057581


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


349 Ore. 10; 239 P.3d 232; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 654


March 2, 2010, Argued and submitted 
September 10, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Partain, 2011 Ore. LEXIS 85 (Or., Feb. 3, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 03P3038; CA A132336. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* On appeal from the Polk County Circuit Court, William M. Horner, Judge. 228 Ore. App. 329, 208 P.3d 526 (2009).


State v. Partain, 228 Ore. App. 329, 208 P.3d 526, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 460 (2009)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part on different grounds. The judgment of the circuit court is vacated, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for resentencing in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Oregon Court of Appeals vacated the 420-month sentence that the Polk County Circuit Court had imposed on defendant in a sex abuse case. On defendant's appeal from the second sentence, the court of appeals reversed. The State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the rule that when a defendant was retried after a successful appeal of his or her conviction, the court in the second proceeding may not impose a harsher sentence than had been imposed in the original proceeding, did not apply, or had to be abandoned. The supreme court agreed with the court of appeals in that nothing about Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.222(5)(a) suggested that the statute was specifically directed at the holding in Turner. The supreme court found that whatever the possibility of vindictive sentencing, the legislature remained competent to deal with it explicitly and, if it did not do so, both the state and federal constitutions provided the appropriate legal backstop. State v. Turner, 247 Ore. 301, 313, 429 P2d 197 (1967) was overruled. Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.020 did not support the idea that the legislature intended to grant to trial judges the authority to use their sentencing power to impose more lengthy sentences on remand for the purpose of punishing defendants for bringing appeals. The trial court could impose a harsher sentence after retrial or remand, as long as its grounds for doing so were not directed at punishing the defendant for appealing.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed on different grounds; the judgment of the circuit court was vacated, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for resentencing.

State v. Moyer


CA A128796 (Control), CA A128797, CA A128798, SC S056990


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


348 Ore. 220; 230 P.3d 7; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 277


September 18, 2009, Argued and Submitted 
April 29, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Moyer v. Oregon, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 6215 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 0409-35104; CC 0409-35105; CC 0409-35106. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, John A. Wittmayer, Judge. 225 Ore. App. 81, 200 P3d 619 (2009).


State v. Moyer, 225 Ore. App. 81, 200 P.3d 619, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 16 (2009)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with violating Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.402 (2003) for making a campaign contribution in another name. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer to the indictment on the ground that the statute violated the free expression clauses of Or. Const. art. I, § 8 and the First Amendment of the United States Constitution. The Court of Appeals of Oregon reversed. Defendants' petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with violating Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.402 for making campaign contributions in the name of persons other than the contributor. Defendants demurred to the indictment, claimed the statute violated free speech under Or. Const. art. I, § 8 and the First Amendment. The Court of Appeals of Oregon held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.402 did not violate free speech. The Supreme Court of Oregon upheld the decision. Prohibiting the concealment of the identity of the true provider of a political contribution was a modern variant of the principle underlying the historic legal prohibition against deceptive or misleading expression. Because Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.402 fell within a historical exception, the statute did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 8. Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.402 gave adequate notice that it was unlawful to make a contribution using another person's name; therefore, defendants' vagueness challenge was rejected.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals was affirmed. The judgment of the trial court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Vannatta v. Or. Gov't Ethics Comm'n


SC S057570


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


348 Ore. 117; 228 P.3d 574; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 265


January 21, 2010, Submitted 
April 8, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 07C20464; CA A140080. On plaintiffs-appellants petition for attorney fees and costs. *


* 347 Ore. 449, 222 P3d 1077 (2009).


Vannatta v. Or. Gov't Ethics Comm'n, 347 Ore. 449, 222 P.3d 1077, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 1025 (2009)



DISPOSITION: Petitioners' statement of costs and disbursements is allowed in the sum of $ 1,053.00. Petitioners' petition for attorney fees is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An Oregon trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, Oregon Government Ethics Commission and the State of Oregon (state) on petitioner voters' claim for declaratory and injunctive relief based on challenges to statutory restrictions on the solicitation, offering, and receipt of gifts and entertainment by public officials and private persons. The supreme court reversed in part. The voters sought costs and attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: The voters argued that they should be awarded attorney fees based on the supreme court's power in equity, a so-called Deras award. The supreme court agreed with the parties that this was a proceeding in equity and that the first Deras requirement was satisfied. Although the voters succeeded in only one of their challenges to the gift and entertainment restriction statutes, the supreme court's opinion reversed the trial court judgment and held that the voters were entitled to a declaratory judgment that the restrictions on offering gifts and entertainment violated their free expression right under Or. Const. art. I, § 8. The voters obtained a substantial modification of the judgment regardless of whether the public officials involved ultimately could accept the offers made. The supreme court designated the voters as the prevailing party. Although the voters established the right to offer gifts and free entertainment to public officials, the supreme court simultaneously upheld the statute prohibiting public officials from accepting them. The net result was not such a significant public benefit that the public at large should pay the voters' attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The voters' statement of costs and disbursements was allowed in the sum of $1,053.00; their petition for attorney fees was denied.

Datt v. Hill


SC S056842


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


347 Ore. 672; 227 P.3d 714; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 58


September 18, 2009, Argued and Submitted 
February 11, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 05024238M; CA A130434. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Malheur County Circuit Court, C. Gregory West, Judge. 220 Ore. App. 657, 188 P3d 384 (2008).


Datt v. Hill, 220 Ore. App. 657, 188 P.3d 384, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 877 (2008)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the judgment of the Malheur County Circuit Court, Oregon, denying his petition for post-conviction relief without providing specific reasons. The Court of Appeals of Oregon interpreted Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.640(1) to require that a post-conviction trial court provide an explanation sufficient to enable the appellate court to determine whether the post-conviction court committed a legal error. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of sexual abuse and other crimes. He sought post-conviction relief under the Oregon Post-Conviction Hearing Act, Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.510 to 138.680, alleging the ineffective assistance of trial counsel. Using a standardized three-paragraph form, the post-conviction court issued its judgment denying relief. The form indicated that the denial was based on: "failure of proof." Petitioner sent a letter, objecting that the judgment did not clearly state the grounds on which the case was determined. The Court of Appeals of Oregon erred by interpreting Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.640(1) to require that a post-conviction court provide an explanation sufficient to enable the appellate court to determine whether the post-conviction court committed a legal error that affected the result. The Supreme Court of Oregon found that the judgment did not clearly state the legal basis for the court's denial of each claim for relief. The judgment did not indicate whether petitioner failed to prove counsel's inadequacy or that he was prejudiced by counsel's performance. Therefore, the judgment failed to meet the standard set forth in Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.640(1).


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals of Oregon was affirmed to the extent that it found that the post-conviction court's judgment was inadequate and reversed as to the Court of Appeals' interpretation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.640(1). The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

State v. Rodgers


SC S056239 (Control), SC S056237


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


347 Ore. 610; 227 P.3d 695; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 61

February 11, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC CM0420629; CA A128857; CC CR030112; CA A128263. (Cases Consolidated for Opinion). On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Benton County Circuit Court, David B. Connell, Judge. 219 Ore. App. 366, 182 P.3d 209 (2008).

Appeal from Yamhill Circuit Court, Ronald W. Stone, Judge. 220 Ore. App. 177, 185 P.3d 510 (2008).


State v. Rodgers, 219 Ore. App. 366, 182 P.3d 209, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 498 (2008)
State v. Kirkeby, 220 Ore. App. 177, 185 P.3d 510, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 700 (2008)



DISPOSITION: In State v. Michael K. Rodgers, S056239, the decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings. In State v. Anthony Douglas Kirkeby, S056237, the decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two consolidated criminal cases where defendants were charged with drug-related offenses, the Court of Appeals of Oregon concluded that the officers' conduct unreasonably extended the duration of the traffic stops in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 9. The State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Both defendants were detained for traffic stops. The officers extended the traffic stop beyond a reasonable time by asking defendants about the contents of their vehicles instead of issuing a traffic citation. Each defendant was unlawfully seized in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 9, because the police conduct was not justified by reasonable suspicion of criminal activity; was unrelated to the traffic violation or issuance of a citation; and significantly restricted each defendant's freedom of movement. After the police found drugs either during a consensual vehicle search or a consensual pat-down search, defendants were charged with controlled substances offenses. Because there were no intervening circumstances or other circumstances mitigating the effect of the illegal seizures of each defendant, the Supreme Court of Oregon concluded that each defendant's consent was the product of police conduct that violated Or. Const. art. I, § 9. The evidence obtained during the searches had to be suppressed.


OUTCOME: The decisions of the Oregon Court of Appeals and the circuit court were affirmed.

State v. Machuca


SC S057910


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


347 Ore. 644; 227 P.3d 729; 2010 Ore. LEXIS 60


December 16, 2009, Argued and Submitted

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 050647097; CA A133362. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Nely L. Johnson, Judge. 231 Ore. App. 232, 218 P3d 145 (2009).


State v. Machuca, 231 Ore. App. 232, 218 P.3d 145, 2009 Ore. App. LEXIS 1507 (2009)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State petitioned for review of a decision of the Oregon Court of Appeals that reversed and remanded defendant's DUII conviction.


OVERVIEW: After a single-car collision, defendant was validly arrested for DUII. At the hospital, an officer accurately informed him of his rights and read the implied consent form setting out the consequences for his refusal to consent to having his blood drawn. Defendant agreed to take the test. The circuit court did not err by refusing to suppress the test results showing that his blood-alcohol content was.20. The State's evidence was sufficient to establish an exigency justifying the warrantless seizure of defendant's blood for constitutional purposes, and the Oregon Court of Appeals erred in concluding otherwise. The Supreme Court of Oregon held that to the extent that defendant's decision to permit the blood draw was influenced by the statutory advice of rights and adverse consequences of refusing the blood draw, the implied consent law operated exactly as the legislature intended.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals was reversed. The judgment of the circuit court was affirmed.

Vannatta v. Or. Gov't Ethics Comm'n


SC S057570


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


347 Ore. 449; 222 P.3d 1077; 2009 Ore. LEXIS 1025


August 13, 2009, Argued and Submitted 
December 31, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at, Petition denied by Vannatta v. Or. Gov't Ethics Comm'n, 348 Ore. 117, 228 P.3d 574, 2010 Ore. LEXIS 265 (2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by VanNatta v. Or Gov't Ethics Comm'n, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 3919 (U.S., May 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 07C20464; CA A140080. On certified review from the Court of Appeals on appeal from a judgment of the Circuit Court for Marion County, Joseph C. Guimond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the circuit court is affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs brought an action for declaratory relief, asserting that Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 244.025(1)-(4) and 244.042, which regulated the solicitation, offering, and receipt of gifts by certain public and other persons, violated Or. Const. art. I, §§ 8 and 26, and the First Amendment and were unenforceable. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of defendant State. The Oregon Court of Appeals certified review to the instant court.


OVERVIEW: The instant court concluded that the trial court correctly granted summary judgment to the State on plaintiffs' challenges to the receipt of gifts and payment of expenses for entertainment restrictions under Or. Const. art. I, § 8 and 26, and the First Amendment. Restrictions on public officials receiving gifts withstood constitutional challenge because giving gifts to public officials was nonexpressive conduct. The fact that gifts could be helpful in creating goodwill with public officials did not mean that Or. Const. art. § I, § 26, protected the delivery of gifts to them. The trial court also correctly granted summary judgment to the State on plaintiffs' challenges to the solicitation of gifts restrictions because plaintiffs did not qualify as persons affected by those restrictions. However, the trial court erred in granting summary judgment to the State regarding plaintiffs' challenges to the restrictions on offering gifts and entertainment under Or. Const. art. I, § 8. Such restrictions impermissibly restricted the right of free expression. Plaintiffs were entitled to a declaratory judgment that the restrictions on offering gifts violated their right to free speech.


OUTCOME: The order of the circuit court was affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

State v. Rodriguez


SC S055720, SC S055721


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


347 Ore. 46; 217 P.3d 659; 2009 Ore. LEXIS 287


November 4, 2008, Argued and submitted 
September 24, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC C051244CR; CA A131050. CC 04102314; CA A131973. (Cases consolidated for argument and opinion). On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Washington County Circuit Court, Nancy W. Campbell, Judge. 217 Ore. App. 351, 174 P.3d 1100 (2007).

Appeal from Linn County Circuit Court, Rick J. McCormick, Judge. 217 Ore. App. 363, 174 P.3d 1106 (2007).


State v. Buck, 217 Ore. App. 363, 174 P.3d 1106, 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1862 (2007)
State v. Rodriguez, 217 Ore. App. 351, 174 P.3d 1100, 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1863 (2007)



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the Court of Appeals are affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgments of the circuit courts are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In cases consolidated for appeal, two defendants were convicted of first-degree sexual abuse under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.427(1)(a)(A). The trial courts held that the mandatory sentences required under Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.700(2)(a)(P) violated Or. Const. art. I, § 16, and imposed lesser sentences. The Court of Appeals of Oregon affirmed the convictions, but held that the trial courts should have imposed the maximum sentences. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: One defendant's conviction was affirmed where the testimony supported the jury's conclusion that she had acted with a sexual purpose in holding the back of the victim's head against her clothed breasts, while massaging the sides of his head. Although the touching itself did not necessarily demonstrate a sexual purpose, the record disclosed a litany of improper communications and conduct between defendant and the boy. The other defendant's conviction was also affirmed where defendant's comments and thoughts were sufficient to permit the factfinder to infer that he had acted with a sexual purpose when he briefly touched a girl. However, the statutorily prescribed penalty under Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.700(2)(a)(P) was so disproportionate to the offenses committed by these defendants that it shocked the moral sense of reasonable people. Thus, the required 75-month sentence would have violated Or. Const. art. I, § 16. Although the required sentence was adopted by the people, acting through the initiative process, and facial challenges had been rejected, its application in these particular cases was unconstitutional. Thus, the trial courts correctly imposed lesser sentences.


OUTCOME: The lower court's decisions were affirmed as to the conclusion that sufficient evidence supported defendants' convictions, but were reversed as to the conclusion that the trial courts should have imposed the mandatory sentences. The judgments of conviction and the sentences imposed by the trial courts were affirmed.

OVERVIEW: The causes of the complaint arose out of the attorney's handling of an estate matter, an employment matter, and complaints that were filed with the Bar around the time of the attorney's suspension. As to the attorney's representation of clients around the time of the attorney's suspension, the attorney falsely represented in his several motions to abate and postpone that his disciplinary matter was on appeal. The trial panel determined that the attorney violated several duties in an intentional or knowing manner. There were also several aggravating factors with only one potential mitigating factor.

Young v. State


SC S056376


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


346 Ore. 507; 212 P.3d 1258; 2009 Ore. LEXIS 37


May 18, 2009, Argued and Submitted 
July 16, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 97C10933; CA A133123. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Paul J. Lipscomb, Judge. 221 Ore. App. 146, 188 P3d 476 (2008).


Young v. State, 221 Ore. App. 146, 188 P.3d 476, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 947 (2008)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a class of state employees, brought an action against defendant State in 1997 seeking unpaid overtime compensation. The trial court entered judgment for the employees. On an earlier appeal, the court ordered a recalculation of the employees' awards. The trial court recalculated the awards and denied post-judgment interest. The employees appealed, and the Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed. The employees again appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employees argued that they were entitled to post-judgment interest on their awards dating from the trial court's original judgment in their favor. The State argued that it was immune from the payment of post-judgment interest. The court held that the State was not immune. When the State submitted to liability for unpaid wages under former Or. Rev. Stat. § 279.340(1) (1995), renumbered as Or. Rev. Stat. § 653.268(1) (2003), and Or. Rev. Stat. § 652.200 (1995), it was responsible for attendant money awards, including attorney fees and costs, which were not distinct claims for damages. The court saw no reason to distinguish between the State's liability for attorney fees and costs and the State's liability for post-judgment interest under Or. Rev. Stat. § 82.010(2)(a). The court held that the supplemental judgments that the trial court eventually entered in accordance with the order to recalculate the awards constituted a modification of the original judgment and did not constitute a full reversal, wiping out the original judgment. Therefore, the post-judgment interest accrued from the date of the original judgment.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals was reversed. The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for an award of post-judgment interest.

State ex rel. Dewberry v. Kulongoski


SC S056410


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


346 Ore. 260; 210 P.3d 884; 2009 Ore. LEXIS 28


February 25, 2009, Argued and Submitted 
June 18, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Decision reached on appeal by, Sub nomine at State ex rel. Dewberry v. Kitzhaber, 2013 Ore. App. LEXIS 1363 (Or. Ct. App., Nov. 14, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CA A124001; CC 16-03-23044. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Lane County Circuit Court, Karsten H. Rasmussen, Judge. 220 Ore. App. 345, 187 P.3d 220 (2008).


State ex rel. Dewberry v. Kulongoski, 220 Ore. App. 345, 187 P.3d 220, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 791 (2008)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents on review, a town's residents, sought a writ of mandamus before a circuit court, challenging petitioner on review, the Governor. On the state's motion, the circuit court dismissed the petition. The residents immediately appealed to the Court of Appeals (Oregon). The intermediate appellate court reversed and remanded. The state's petition for review was allowed.


OVERVIEW: The residents challenged the Governor's authority to enter into a gaming compact with certain Indian Tribes. Under the compact, the Tribes would be permitted to open a casino near the residents' town. The supreme court first addressed whether Or. R. Civ. P. 29 A applied to mandamus proceedings. The circuit court erred in concluding that Rule 29 A required that the residents join the Tribes in the mandamus action. The supreme court next concluded that a declaratory judgment action did not provide a plain, speedy and adequate remedy because under Or. Rev. Stat. § 28.110, the Tribes would be required to participate in any declaratory judgment action. If the Tribes chose not to participate by invoking sovereign immunity, a court would be entirely without authority to decide the case. Moreover, because the Tribes control its availability, a declaratory judgment action would not afford the residents any relief. In this case, however, a declaratory judgment action was neither a plain nor adequate alternative to a mandamus proceeding for compelling the Governor to perform his official duty with regard to the gaming compact.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court affirmed. The case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Baker


CA A132245, SC S055809


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


346 Ore. 1; 202 P.3d 174; 2009 Ore. LEXIS 7


November 4, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
February 12, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Baker, 2010 Ore. App. LEXIS 127 (Or. Ct. App., Feb. 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 05FE0977MS. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* On order of dismissal dated January 24, 2008.


State v. Baker, 344 Ore. 539, 186 P.3d 285, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 333 (2008)



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Appeals is reversed, and the case is remanded to that court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered guilty pleas to second-degree sexual abuse and incest. Defendant appealed his sentences. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) dismissed the appeal. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The trial court sentenced defendant to 180 months in prison on the sexual abuse counts, and it sentenced him to 30 months in prison on the incest counts. Defendant claimed that his sentence was unconstitutionally disproportionate. The State claimed that the issue did not come within the grounds for appeal. The issue turned on a question of statutory construction: did a claim that a sentence was unconstitutionally disproportionate come within the terms of Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.050(1)? The supreme court concluded that the legislature permitted a defendant who entered a guilty plea to appeal on the ground that his sentence was unconstitutionally cruel and unusual; that phrase necessarily included a claim that the sentence was not proportioned to the offense in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The supreme court reached the same conclusion under Or. Const. art. I, § 16. For the purposes of interpreting Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.050(1)(b), the statutory phrase "unconstitutionally cruel and unusual" included a claim that a sentence was not proportioned to the offense in violation of Article I, section 16. Defendant's constitutional proportionality challenge was within § 138.050(1)(b).


OUTCOME: The order of the intermediate appellate court was reversed, and the case was remanded to that court for further proceedings.

Pendleton Sch. Dist. 16R v. State


SC S056096


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


345 Ore. 596; 200 P.3d 133; 2009 Ore. LEXIS 1


October 8, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
January 23, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at Pendleton Sch. Dist. 16R v. State, 2009 Ore. LEXIS 250 (Or., Sept. 17, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 0603-02980; CA A133649. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Christopher J. Marshall, Judge. 220 Ore. App. 56, 185 P.3d 471 (2008).


Pendleton Sch. Dist. 16R v. State, 220 Ore. App. 56, 185 P.3d 471, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 648 (2008)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for entry of a declaratory judgment consistent with this opinion and for a declaration that Article VIII, section 8, of the Oregon Constitution does not authorize the injunctive relief that plaintiffs sought.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sued defendant State, seeking a declaratory judgment that the legislature was constitutionally required to fund the state public school system at a level sufficient to meet certain quality educational goals established by law and an injunction directing the legislature to appropriate the necessary funds. The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment for the State. Plaintiffs petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs contended that Or. Const. art. VIII, § 8, required the legislature to appropriate funds sufficient to maintain an adequate system of K-12 public schools, but the legislature had not appropriated sufficient funds for the 2005-2007 biennium. The instant court concluded that Or. Const. art. VIII, § 3, directed the legislature to provide funding sufficient for the public schools to meet quality goals established by law. The trial court should have entered a declaratory judgment on the limited ground that the legislature failed to fund the public school system at the level specified in Or. Const. art. VIII, § 8. However, the instant court also concluded that plaintiffs were not entitled to a broader declaratory judgment that would require the legislature to fund the public school system in each biennium at the levels required by Or. Const. art. VIII, § 8, as well as an injunction requiring the legislature to fund the public school system at those levels. The instant court also concluded that plaintiffs did not allege any deficiencies in the funding for the public school system that placed it below the standards that Or. Const. art. VIII, § 3, imposed.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgment of the circuit court was affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for entry of a declaratory judgment consistent with the instant court's opinion and for a declaration that Or. Const. art. VIII, § 8, did not authorize the injunctive relief that plaintiffs sought.

 

84 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Johnson


SC S055085


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


345 Ore. 190; 191 P.3d 665; 2008 Ore. LEXIS 598


January 7, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
August 14, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC D0304188M; CA A124900. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Washington County Circuit Court, Jon B. Lund, Senior Judge. 213 Ore. App. 83, 159 P.3d 1213 (2007).


State v. Johnson, 213 Ore. App. 83, 159 P.3d 1213, 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 733 (2007)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted on two counts of harassment in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(a)(B). The Court of Appeals (Oregon) concluded on defendant's appeal that Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(a)(B) was constitutional both on its face and as applied, and affirmed defendant's convictions. The court allowed defendant's petition for review.


OVERVIEW: Even speech that was intended and likely to produce violence could not be criminalized unless the violence was imminent. A harassment statute that lacked that limitation swept too much protected speech within its reach to survive a facial challenge. Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(a)(B) focused on effects that the legislature wished to forbid, harassment or annoyance of another person. Defendant's expression may have been offensive, but the State could not suppress all speech that offended with the club of the criminal law. Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(a)(B) criminalized a harm that resulted only from a kind of expression, public insults through abusive words or gestures. The harm that the statute sought to prevent, harassment or annoyance, generally was one against which the Oregon Constitution did not permit the criminal law to shield individuals when that harm was caused by another's speech. For that reason, the prohibition contained in Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(a)(B) was overbroad on its face and violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals affirming defendant's conviction was reversed. The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Christiansen v. Providence Health Sys. of Or. Corp.


CA A122603


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


344 Ore. 445; 184 P.3d 1121; 2008 Ore. LEXIS 276


January 9, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
May 8, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 0301-00634; SC S054731. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Nely L. Johnson, Judge. 210 Ore. App 290, 150 P.3d 50 (2006).


Christiansen v. Providence Health Sys. of Or. Corp., 210 Ore. App. 290, 150 P.3d 50, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 2017 (2006)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a mother who was the conservator for her minor child, brought a medical negligence complaint against respondent physician and respondent medical center. The trial court dismissed the matter on the ground that the action was barred by the five-year limitation period established in Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.110(4). The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The complaint alleged that the physician and the hospital negligently caused injuries to the child while the mother was in labor. Among other things, the mother alleged that the physician and the hospital failed to recognize the signs of fetal distress, and they delayed in performing a caesarean-section delivery. At two and three months after the child's birth, a neurologist informed the mother that the child was developing normally. Later, the same neurologist diagnosed the child with mixed developmental disorder, developmental speech, or language disorder. On appeal, the mother argued that application of Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.110(4) violated the remedy requirement set forth in Or. Const. art. I, § 10. The supreme court disagreed with the mother's argument that legislature had abolished a pre-statehood common-law right. The pre-statehood disability law did not protect the mother from the five-year limitation period in § 12.110(4). The remedy clause set forth in Or. Const. art. I, § 10 did not prevent legislative enactment of a statute that modified the disability protection that minors enjoyed before 1859.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court and the judgment of the circuit court were affirmed.

Corey v. Dep't of Land Conservation & Dev.


CA A129905


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


344 Ore. 457; 184 P.3d 1109; 2008 Ore. LEXIS 274


February 27, 2008, Argued and Submitted 
May 8, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Dismissed by, Moot Corey v. Dep't of Land Conservation & Dev., 221 Ore. App. 505, 190 P.3d 493, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1207 (Or. Ct. App., Aug. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
DLCD M119478; SC S054995. On petition for review and motion to vacate and remand. * 


* On review of the decision of the Court of Appeals. 210 Ore. App 542, 152 P.3d 933, adh'd to, 212 Ore. App 536, 159 P.3d 327 (2007).


Corey v. Dep't of Land Conservation & Dev., 212 Ore. App. 536, 159 P.3d 327, 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 669 (2007)



DISPOSITION: The petition for review is dismissed as moot. The motion to vacate and remand is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: From a land use case, petitioner Department of Land Conservation and Development (DLCD) sought review of a prior decision decided under Ballot Measure 37. The DLCD subsequently contended that the Ballot Measure 49 rendered the prior decision moot and requested that its petition seeking review be rendered moot and the prior decision be vacated. Respondent landowner parties in the prior decision denied that that decision was moot.


OVERVIEW: On review, the DLCD contended that Measure 49 (codified at Or. Rev. Stat. § 195.305) extinguished all claims and orders under Measure 37 (codified at Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.352 (2005)). The supreme court agreed that the prior decision was moot as Measure 49 rendered the waiver order at the center of the jurisdictional dispute wholly ineffective. As to the landowners' contention, the supreme court determined that Measure 49 provided them with an opportunity to assert their claim, and a forum in which they could assert it. Whether the Oregon Court of Appeals or the circuit court had jurisdiction to review the DLCD's final order respecting the landowners' 37 claim could have no practical effect upon the parties: if the order at issue had no continuing legal effect, then neither party could gain anything from review in either forum. Thus, the prior case was moot. Contrary to the landowners' argument, Or. Rev. Stat. § 14.175 did not present a basis for review because the landowners could pursue their claim pursuant to the procedure set out in Measure 49, including any assertion that Measure 49 acted on them in an unconstitutional way.


OUTCOME: The petition for review was dismissed as moot. The motion to vacate and remand was denied.

Goddard v. Farmers Ins. Co.


SC S053405


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


344 Ore. 232; 179 P.3d 645; 2008 Ore. LEXIS 151


January 4, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
March 6, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Goddard v. Farmers Ins. Co., 2008 Ore. LEXIS 423 (Or., May 29, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC No. 9005-03204; CA A118750. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Douglas G. Beckman, Judge. 202 Ore. App. 79, 120 P.3d 1260 (2005), modified and adh'd to as modified on recons, 203 Ore. App. 744, 126 P.3d 682 (2006).


Goddard v. Farmers Ins. Co., 203 Ore. App. 744, 126 P.3d 682, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 32 (2006)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed as modified by this opinion. The judgment of the circuit court is vacated and remanded with instructions to grant defendant's motion for new trial, limited to punitive damages, unless plaintiff agrees to remittitur of punitive damages to four times compensatory damages award.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner decedent's mother sued respondent driver's insurer for wrongful death. A jury found for the mother and awarded punitive damages based on bad faith failure to settle her third party claim. The trial court reduced the compensatory damages award, but left the punitive damage award undisturbed. The Court of Appeals of Oregon reinstated the compensatory damage award, but significantly reduced the punitive damage award. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The insurer's arguments unrelated to punitive damages were not considered as review had been allowed review to consider a particular set of issues. Under relevant federal case law, three guideposts determined whether a punitive damage award violated the Due Process Clause: reprehensibility, the ratio between punitive damages and the actual or potential harm to the plaintiff, and the relationship between the punitive damage award and civil or criminal penalties for comparable conduct. The second guidepost, the ratio, provided a rough numerical baseline for calculating a maximum constitutionally permissible award in a given case. Case law and Or. Rev. Stat. § 746.230 put the insurer was on notice that its refusal to settle the mother's claim against the driver within policy limits was not immune from punitive damages. Under the guideposts, the insurer's conduct was very reprehensible as it was directed at a financially vulnerable victim, was not confined to the victim, and involved intentional malice and deceit. However, the punitive damages was more than case law allowed as they were 16 times the potential harm. The maximum punitive damage award was four times the potential harm.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed as modified to reflect that the highest permissible punitive damage award was four times the amount of the mother's actual and potential harm. The trial court's judgment was vacated and remanded with instructions to grant the insurer's motion for a new trial, limited to punitive damages, unless the mother agreed to remittitur of punitive damages to four times the compensatory damages award.
