Hughes v. PeaceHealth


SC S053447


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


344 Ore. 142; 178 P.3d 225; 2008 Ore. LEXIS 60


November 1, 2006, Argued and Submitted 
February 22, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Hughes v. Peacehealth, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 358 (Or., May 7, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 16-02-18544; CA A123782. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Lane County Circuit Court, Maurice K. Merten, Judge. 204 Ore. App. 614, 131 P.3d 798 (2006).


Hughes v. Peacehealth, 204 Ore. App. 614, 131 P.3d 798, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 319 (2006)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner personal representative challenged a judgment from the Oregon Court of Appeals, which affirmed a lower court's decision to cap the damages awarded in a wrongful death case filed against respondent medical providers.


OVERVIEW: The jury entered an award of one million dollars in noneconomic damages in a wrongful death action. The trial court applied Or. Rev. Stat. § 31.710 and reduced the award to $500,000. It also reduced the interest under Or. Rev. Stat. § 82.010(2)(f). The court of appeals affirmed, and this appeal followed. In affirming, the supreme court determined that there was no violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 10 because a wrongful death claim was not recognized at common law when this constitutional provision was adopted. Moreover, the common law of Oregon did not incorporate Lord Campbell's Act, 9 & 10 Vict, ch 93 (1846) because it was enacted after the American Revolution. Therefore, the statutory damages cap in Or. Rev. Stat. § 31.701 did not abolish a remedy that was available at common law when Or. Const. art. I, § 10 was drafted. Next, the right to a jury trial under Or. Const. art. I, § 17 could not have conferred a right to a jury award of a kind or amount that was contrary to statutory law. Finally, the lower interest rate in Or. Rev. Stat. § 82.0101(2)(f) was applicable here because the term "injuries" contained therein referred to any violation of a legal right.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

State v. Jacob


SC S054173


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


344 Ore. 181; 180 P.3d 6; 2008 Ore. LEXIS 59


September 10, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
February 22, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 020231253; CA A119971. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Robert Redding, Senior Judge. 208 Ore. App. 62, 145 P.3d 212 (2006).


State v. Jacob, 208 Ore. App. 62, 145 P.3d 212, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 1467 (2006)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted three times of felonies involving the use or threatened use of firearms. The trial court that sentenced defendant on the third felony refused to impose a 30-year minimum sentence pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.610(4)(c) (2001). The State appealed. The Court of Appeals, Oregon, reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the case to the trial court for imposition of sentence.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that defendant could not collaterally attack the first conviction. On review, defendant argued that the terms of Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.610 (2001) did not permit imposition of a 30-year gun minimum sentence. The instant court found that defendant's third conviction met both of the requirements of § 161.610(4)(c) because (1) the crime for which defendant was convicted was a felony, robbery in the first degree, which had as an element the use of a deadly weapon, with the indictment specifying a firearm as that weapon, and (2) it followed his imprisonment for 10 years pursuant to § 161.610(4)(b). Thus, the trial court was required to impose a 30-year minimum sentence. The instant court declined to hold that defendant's first conviction was invalid because it could not say that the first conviction deprived defendant of his right to a jury trial, and defendant could not pursue, more than 20 years later, a collateral attack on his first conviction. Finally, regardless of the validity of the first conviction, the terms of § 161.610(4)(c) required the imposition of a 30-year gun minimum sentence.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

State v. Wheeler


SC S054543

343 Ore. 652; 175 P.3d 438; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 1231

Clarke v. Or. Health Scis. Univ.


SC S053868


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


343 Ore. 581; 175 P.3d 418; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 1233


January 9, 2007, Argued and Submitted 
December 28, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 0005-05116; CA A124560. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Henry Kantor,Judge. 206 Ore. App. 610, 138 P.3d 900 (2006).


Clarke v. Or. Health Scis. Univ., 206 Ore. App. 610, 138 P.3d 900, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 937 (2006)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient sued defendant Oregon Health Sciences University (OHSU) and individuals who treated him, alleging negligence. Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 30.265(1) and 30.270(1), OHSU was substituted as the sole defendant and judgment on the pleadings was entered against OHSU. The Court of Appeals of Oregon concluded that the substitution of OHSU for the individuals violated Or. Const. art. I, § 10. OHSU and the individuals appealed.


OVERVIEW: The primary issue was whether Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 30.265(1) and 30.270(1), as applied to facts of this case, violated Or. Const. art. I, § 10. OHSU would have enjoyed sovereign immunity at common law where its function, powers, and governance structure indicated that it was an instrumentality of the state performing state functions. The Oregon Tort Claims Act's 1991 elimination of a cause of action against the individuals, combined with its limitation of damages, did not survive scrutiny under Or. Const. art. I, § 10, where, at the time Oregon adopted its remedy guarantee, the patient would have been entitled to seek and recover economic and noneconomic damages. The 1991 amendments to the statute eliminated that right by imposing capped remedies against the public body only. Although the legislature was authorized under Or. Const. art. I, § 10, to vary or modify the nature, form, or amount of recovery for a common-law remedy, it could not emasculate the common-law remedy.


OUTCOME: The lower court's decision was affirmed. The circuit court's judgment was reversed. The case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

 

92 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. McDonnell


SC S049368


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


343 Ore. 557; 176 P.3d 1236; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 1209


November 6, 2006, Argued and Submitted 
December 19, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. McDonnell, 2008 Ore. LEXIS 219 (Or., Mar. 26, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McDonnell v. Oregon, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5758 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC J850004. On automatic and direct review of the judgment of conviction and sentence of death imposed by the Circuit Court of Douglas County. Robert C. Millikan, Judge.
State v. McDonnell, 329 Ore. 375, 987 P.2d 486, 1999 Ore. LEXIS 600 (1999)



DISPOSITION: The sentence of death is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In 1988, defendant was convicted of aggravated murder in the Circuit Court of Douglas County, Oregon, and sentenced to death. The conviction was vacated and, on remand, defendant was again found guilty of aggravated murder and sentenced to death. The conviction was affirmed, but the sentence was vacated. Another death sentence was vacated in 1999 and, afterward, in 2002, defendant was again sentenced to death. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: In the initial trial, the trial judge was disqualified on defendant's objection under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 14.250 and 14.260. The same judge was assigned to preside over the 2002 penalty-phase proceedings. Neither party objected. Although Or. Rev. Stat. § 14.250 and the 1987 disqualification order prevented the judge from further participation in the case, his conduct in presiding over the 2002 penalty-phase trial was a procedural error. Because defendant did not object when the judge presided over the 2002 penalty-phase proceeding, he did not preserve the issue for review. During the 2002 penalty-phase proceeding, the admission of transcripts of prior witness testimony under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.012(a)(b) and Or. Evid. Code 804(3)(a), did not violate defendant's rights to confrontation under U.S. Const. amend. VI and Or. Const. art. I, § 11. Defendant did not object to the State's introduction of the transcripts and thus did not preserve the issue for review. A State expert's testimony on rebuttal examination related to the second question in Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.150(1)(b), often referred to as the "future dangerousness question," and did not exceed the scope of proper rebuttal.


OUTCOME: The sentence of death was affirmed.

 

93 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sanders


SC S53056


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


343 Ore. 35; 163 P.3d 607; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 582


September 6, 2006, Argued and Submitted 
June 21, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 011137484; CA A122330. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Jerome LaBarre, Judge. 202 Ore. App. 672, 126 P.3d 1260 (2005).


State v. Sanders, 202 Ore. App. 672, 126 P.3d 1260, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1535 (2005)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.076, the trial court entered an order requiring defendant, who had been convicted of the felony offense of fleeing or attempting to elude a police officer in a motor vehicle, to submit a blood or buccal sample as a condition of his probation. The Oregon Court of Appeals upheld the trial court's order. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the requirement in Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.076 that he provide a blood or buccal sample violated his state and federal constitutional rights to be free from unreasonable searches and seizures. The court concluded that the requirement in § 137.076 that persons convicted of felonies provide a blood or buccal sample as a condition of probation does not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 9. The statute does not necessarily deprive felony offenders of certain of their constitutional rights in general, or of their rights under Or. Const. art. I, § 9, in particular, by subjecting them to a specific form of search and seizure as a consequence of their convictions. The statute narrows the class of persons potentially subject to it by limiting its terms to felons and persons convicted of certain serious offenses. The court perceived no principled basis for declaring that blood and buccal samples are different in any constitutionally significant way from gathering or retaining a person's identity through fingerprints and photographs. The court also concluded that requiring convicted felons to submit a blood or buccal sample does not violate the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court and the judgment of the circuit court were affirmed.

 

94 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Birchfield


SC S53363


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


342 Ore. 624; 157 P.3d 216; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 354


November 6, 2006, Argued and Submitted 
April 19, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 021254735; CA A123284. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Clifford J. Freeman, Judge. 204 Ore. App 689, 131 P.3d 804 (2006).


State v. Birchfield, 204 Ore. App. 689, 131 P.3d 804, 2006 Ore. App. LEXIS 309 (2006).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State charged defendant with attempted possession of a controlled substance. The trial court, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.235 and prior case law, allowed the State to introduce a laboratory report without calling a criminalist. Defendant was convicted of attempted possession of a controlled substance, and he appealed. The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed defendant's conviction. Defendant's petition for review was allowed.


OVERVIEW: A police officer arrested defendant for driving under the influence of intoxicants and searched defendant. The officer discovered a kind of glass pipe used to smoke cocaine. A criminalist at the laboratory analyzed the pipe's contents and returned a written report stating that the pipe contained cocaine residue. At issue in this case was whether the admission of a laboratory report at defendant's criminal trial violated his right to meet the witnesses face to face under Or. Const. art. I, § 11, or his right to be confronted with the witnesses against him under the Sixth Amendment. The trial court's admission of the laboratory report, without requiring the state to produce at trial the criminalist who prepared the report or to demonstrate that the criminalist was unavailable to testify, violated defendant's right to confront the witness against him under Or. Const. art. I, § 11. Prior case law had essentially equated the notice and subpoena concepts. The supreme court concluded the previously established subpoena requirement was erroneous. The supreme court did not need to reach the question of whether the admission of the laboratory report also violated the Sixth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court was reversed. The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Carey v. Lincoln Loan Co.


SC S53242


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


342 Ore. 530; 157 P.3d 775; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 346


November 14, 2006, Submitted on the Record  
April 12, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Carey v. Lincoln Loan Co., 2007 Ore. LEXIS 556 (Or., June 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 9706-04753; CA A117696. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Henry Kantor, Judge. 203 Ore. App 399, 125 P.3d 814 (2005).


Carey v. Lincoln Loan Co., 203 Ore. App. 399, 125 P.3d 814, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1674 (2005)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs buyers sued defendant seller and argued that certain provisions in the parties' land sale contract violated the law, and the buyers' limited ability to prepay the contract was impermissible. After the Oregon Court of Appeals reversed a trial court judgment in favor of the seller, the seller appealed and asserted that the Court of Appeals was not lawfully constituted under Or. Const. art. VII (Amended).


OVERVIEW: The seller argued that the Court of Appeals was not lawfully constituted under Or. Const. art. VII (Amended), because - in the seller's view - the provision of the Oregon Constitution that purported to authorize the legislature to establish courts was improperly adopted in 1910. It therefore followed that, when the legislature created the Court of Appeals by statute in 1969, it had no authority to do so. In particular, the seller alleged that without Art. VII (Amended) in place, the legislature had lacked the authority to create the Court of Appeals because Article VII (Original), which established the judicial power of the state, did not grant the legislature that authority. The instant court concluded that the irregularities, if any, in the adoption of Art. VII in 1910 had been cured by the implicit subsequent validation of that article by the people. Therefore that Art. VII was valid, and it followed that the creation of the Court of Appeals in 1969, pursuant to the authority granted by Art. VII , § 1, was within the legislature's authority. The Court of Appeals had jurisdiction over the buyers' appeal in the present case.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals was affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court was reversed and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Fidanque v. Myers


SC S54310


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


342 Ore. 485; 155 P.3d 867; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 195


February 27, 2007, Submitted on the Record  
March 23, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Fidanque v. Myers, 342 Ore. 634, 2007 Ore. LEXIS 584 (2007)



DISPOSITION: Ballot title referred to Attorney General for modification.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a ballot title review proceeding, petitioner electors challenged respondent attorney general's certified ballot title for a proposed initiative measure that would amend the Oregon Constitution to permit local governments and the state to use their regulatory authority to restrict any strip act, to the extent such regulation was allowed under the U.S. Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The electors argued that the "yes" vote result statement did not adequately describe the "result" if the proposed initiative was approved because the statement failed to mention that adoption of the measure would override the guarantee in Or. Const. art. I, § 8 of freedom from laws restricting expressive activity. The attorney general responded that the result of the proposed measure was not to "limit" Oregon's free-expression provision or to overturn any particular court decisions striking down ordinances or statutes under § 8, but to allow state and local governments to use their "regulatory authority" to restrict strip acts, to the extent allowed under the federal constitution. The supreme court agreed with the electors, holding that the "yes" vote result statement had to state, in some way, that the initiative would change the free expression right protected by the Oregon Constitution if the measure were approved. Because the "yes" vote result statement did not adequately describe that result, it had to be modified.


OUTCOME: The ballot title was referred to the attorney general for modification.

Bailey v. Lampert


SC S53154


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


342 Ore. 321; 153 P.3d 95; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 63


November 6, 2006, Argued and Submitted  
February 8, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 02-05-201M; CA A124795. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Malheur County Circuit Court, Joseph F. Ceniceros, Senior Judge. 203 Ore. App. 45, 125 P.3d 771 (2005).


Bailey v. Lampert, 203 Ore. App. 45, 125 P.3d 771, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1567 (2005)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A trial court granted petitioner inmate's petition for postconviction relief from his convictions in 2000 of two counts of being a felon in possession of a firearm in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.270. The Court of Appeals of Oregon reversed. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted in 2000 of being a felon in possession of a firearm based on a conviction in 1995 of first-degree felony sexual penetration under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.411. When that conviction was reversed, he sought postconviction relief from his 2000 firearm convictions. On appeal of the appellate court's decision that the inmate was not entitled to such relief, the court affirmed. The court held that because at the time the inmate possessed the firearms, he had been convicted of a felony and no court had overturned or set aside that conviction, the inmate's conduct fell within the statutory definitions set out in § 166.270. Section 166.270 did not make any provision for setting aside the inmate's convictions on the ground that the predicate felony conviction was later overturned. The court rejected the inmate's challenge to his convictions under Or. Const. art. I, § 11 and the Standerfer case because the inmate's prior felony conviction was not a fact for the court to consider at sentencing but rather was an element of the crime for which he was convicted, and it was undisputed that he had the status of "felon" at the time he possessed the firearm.


OUTCOME: The judgment reversing the trial court's decision granting the inmate postconviction relief was affirmed.

State v. Hankins


SC S52438


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


342 Ore. 258; 151 P.3d 149; 2007 Ore. LEXIS 1


September 12, 2006, Argued and Submitted  
January 19, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Hankins, 2007 Ore. LEXIS 229 (Or., Mar. 7, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC C002213CR; CA A115703. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Washington County Circuit Court, Suzanne M. Upton, Judge. 194 Ore. App 140, 93 P.3d 826 (2004), on recons, 197 Ore. App 345, 105 P.3d 902 (2005).


State v. Hankins, 194 Ore. App. 140, 93 P.3d 826, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 808 (2004)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An indictment alleged that defendant had committed a felony by delivering marijuana to a minor. At trial, defendant moved for a judgment of acquittal on the ground that the indictment failed to allege all the elements of the charged offense. The trial court denied that motion. The Oregon Court of Appeals agreed and affirmed the trial court's judgment. Defendant's petition for review was allowed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that, under former Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.995(5) (1999), the indictment was deficient because it failed to allege that he was 18 years of age or older and that the victim was at least three years younger than he was. In denying defendant's motion for judgment of acquittal, the trial court reasoned that there was evidence from which a reasonable juror could find both those facts. When defendant objected that he was challenging the sufficiency of the indictment, not the sufficiency of the evidence, the trial court explained that a demurrer was the proper way to challenge the sufficiency of an indictment. The initial question was whether defendant's motion for judgment of acquittal preserved his claim that the indictment failed to allege a violation of § 475.995(5). The trial court did not abuse its discretion in not permitting a mid-trial demurrer. Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.630 set out six grounds for demurrer to an indictment. The trial court did not err in denying the motion for judgment of acquittal. Further, on appeal, defendant assigned error to only the denial of his motion for judgment on acquittal but not to his post-verdict motion based on the same ground.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court and the judgment of the circuit court were affirmed.

Hinton v. Hill


SC S52819


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


342 Ore. 222; 149 P.3d 1205; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 1397


May 8, 2006, Argued and Submitted  
December 29, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Hinton v. Hill, 2007 Ore. LEXIS 90 (Or., Feb. 6, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 01C10035; CA A121726. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Jamese L. Rhoades, Judge. 197 Ore. App. 238, 105 P.3d 923, on recons, 201 Ore. App. 131, 117 P.3d 1066 (2005).


Hinton v. Hill, 197 Ore. App. 238, 105 P.3d 923, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 88 (2005)
Hinton v. Hill, 201 Ore. App. 131, 117 P.3d 1066, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 1023 (2005)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the post-conviction court is affirmed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a petition for review, respondent superintendent challenged the Court of Appeals' (Oregon) holding that a post-conviction court erred in failing to void petitioner prisoner's convictions, arguing that the election to vacate the prisoner's sentences and not her convictions was proper under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.520 of the Post-Conviction Hearing Act and consistent with the post-conviction court's authority under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.530(1)(a).


OVERVIEW: The prisoner pled no contest to six counts of first-degree sexual abuse and was sentenced to 366 months in prison. After the post-conviction court set aside her sentences without vacating the no contest pleas, the court of appeals found that the no contest pleas were involuntary because the prisoner was incorrectly told that her sentence could not exceed 226 months; thus, § 138.530(1) required the vacation of the underlying convictions. However, the court of appeals' interpretation of the phrase "rendered the conviction void" in § 138.530(1)(a) improperly limited the broad authority granted to the post-conviction court, Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.520, to fashion a remedy to correct the actual prejudice the prisoner suffered. The prisoner entered a no contest plea believing that her prison sentence could not exceed 226 months. Thus, the actual prejudice she suffered was limited to the period of incarceration imposed that exceeded 226 months. The post-conviction court's decision to remand the case with instructions to decrease the sentence from 366 months to 226 months or to allow the prisoner to withdraw her no contest pleas was sufficient to remedy the actual prejudice she suffered.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed. The judgment of the post-conviction court was affirmed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Powell v. Bunn


SC S52657, S52659


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


341 Ore. 306; 142 P.3d 1054; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 823


May 3, 2006, Argued and Submitted  
September 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 010403557, CA A117310. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Ellen F. Rosenblum, Judge. 198 Ore. App. 21, 108 P.3d 37 (2005).


Powell v. Bunn, 198 Ore. App. 21, 108 P.3d 37, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 216 (2005).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a school superintendent and a department of education, sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the conclusion of the district court that the superintendent erred in determining the school allowing an organization, in which religion played a part, to make presentations at the public school that plaintiff student attended was not discriminatory.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, the student's mother and the student were atheists, and the school allowed the organization to make presentations to the children during a time and in a place where the children were required to be present. The mother asserted that subjecting the son to the presentations was discriminatory under Or. Rev. Stat. § 659.850 in light of that organization's religious membership criterion and the son's ineligibility to join due to his atheism. The court reversed and held that the fact that the school district permitted a group to provide flyers to be handed out or to make a presentation during lunch period or to include information in a newsletter did not transform all the activities of that group into a public elementary school activity. There was no "differentiated treatment" among the children by those activities. Rather, all children were treated in precisely the same way. Any enrollment in the group was not done by the school district, nor was it done in any public elementary school activity. The school district did nothing more than permit a group to provide nondiscriminatory information to parents and students.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the court of appeals and the circuit court.


 

104 of 770 DOCUMENTS



Urhausen v. City of Eugene


SC S53391


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


341 Ore. 246; 142 P.3d 1023; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 820


June 19, 2006, Argued and Submitted  
August 31, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] TC 4692. On review from the Oregon Tax Court. * 


* Appeal from General Judgment of the Oregon Tax Court. Henry C. Breithaupt, Judge. Urhausen v. City of Eugene, 18 OTR 395 (2006).


Urhausen v. City of Eugene, 18 OTR 395, 2006 Ore. Tax LEXIS 29 (2006)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a decision of the Oregon Tax Court, which concluded that the city's revenue categorization pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 310.155(3) was not consistent with Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b (Measure 5) requirements. In so holding, the tax court declared § 310.155(3) unconstitutional and held that Measure 5 required that revenues be categorized according to their intended use and the purpose for which those revenues were raised.


OVERVIEW: The city passed a resolution calling for a four-year levy to institute a new property tax. The bulk of the levy was aimed at school funding, but the city did not categorize the anticipated levy revenues as public school funds. It categorized those revenues as funds for government operations other than the public school system. The court affirmed and held that because the governmental unit in question was not a school unit, Or. Rev. Stat. § 310.155(3) required that the levy be categorized as funding for "other government" services unless its sole purpose was to fund educational services. Measure 5 required only that the portion of funds that were to be used exclusively for educational services be categorized as funding for "public schools." To the extent that § 310.155(3) limited the categorization of revenues from mixed-use levies to only one category in circumstances such as this, that provision was inconsistent with Measure 5 and therefore was unconstitutional. Any plausible interpretation of the levy's purpose led to the conclusion that the majority of the revenues were raised specifically for the school system. As a result, those revenues had to be categorized accordingly.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the tax court was affirmed.


 

105 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Illig-Renn


SC S52633


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


341 Ore. 228; 142 P.3d 62; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 816


May 4, 2006, Argued and Submitted  
August 24, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC CR0014215; CA A114387. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Clackamas County Circuit Court, Eve Miller, Judge. 196 Ore. App. 765, 103 P.3d 1178 (2004); 199 Ore. App. 124, 110 P.3d 137 (2005).


State v. Illig-Renn, 199 Ore. App. 124, 110 P.3d 137, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 454 (2005)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged a decision from the Oregon Court of Appeals, which affirmed a circuit court's decision to allow defendant's demurrer in a case charging her with interfering with a peace officer under Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) (1999).


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) (1999) was overbroad and vague on its face under the Oregon and United States Constitutions. The circuit court granted the demurrer, and the court of appeals affirmed. The State then sought review. In reversing, the supreme court determined that only statutes that by their terms proscribed the exercise of the constitutionally protected rights of assembly or expression were susceptible to a facial overbreadth challenge under Or. Const. art. I, §§ 8 and 26. Because Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.247(1)(b) did not do such, defendant could have only mounted an "as applied" challenge. Since there was not a substantial amount of protected conduct proscribed by the statute, it was not overbroad under the First and Fourteenth Amendments either. The statute was not vague because the term "lawful order" granted no discretion and no opportunity for unequal application, in violation of Or. Const. art. I, §§ 20, 21. Nor did the statute fail to contain an identifiable standard or fail to provide fair warning, in violation of the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. Finally, the statute was not rendered vague by the addition of an exception.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, as was the decision of the circuit court. The case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Roberts v. SAIF Corp. (In re Roberts)


SC S52078


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


341 Ore. 48; 136 P.3d 1105; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 559


June 22, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
June 15, 2006 In The, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Agency No. 02-07221; CA A122465. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Judicial Review from the Workers' Compensation Board. 196 Ore. App. 414, 102 P.3d 752 (2004).


Roberts v. SAIF Corp. (In re Roberts), 196 Ore. App. 414, 102 P.3d 752, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 1566 (2004)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the order of the Workers' Compensation Board are affirmed. Durham, J., concurred and filed an opinion in which Riggs, J., joined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner workers' compensation claimant sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirming the Workers' Compensation Board's finding that the exclusion of Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.005(7)(b)(B) applied to the claimant's injury that he received at work while riding a coworker's motorcycle. The claimant was sales person for respondent employer, an automobile dealership.


OVERVIEW: The claimant took a ride on a coworker's motorcycle on the sales lot while the sales people were waiting for customers. As he was pulling into a service bay to park it, another employee backed a company pickup into the claimant, severely injuring him. The administrative law judge (ALJ) ruled in favor of the claimant and the Board reversed. The court held that in the context of the case, riding the motorcycle was a "recreational activity." Even though the claimant's injury did not occur as the result of engaging in a recreational activity, it did occur while engaging in that recreational activity. The legislature intended that the Board should determine the degree to which a recreational activity served the employer's work-related interests and the degree to which the worker engaged in the activity for the his personal pleasure. Given that the claimant stipulated that his riding of the motorcycle served no business purpose, substantial evidence supported the Board's finding that there was no work-related reason for claimant to be riding the motorcycle at the time of the accident and that the fundamental or principal reason that he rode the motorcycle was for his personal pleasure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals and the order of the Board.

Lincoln Loan Co. v. City of Portland


SC S51666


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


340 Ore. 613; 136 P.3d 1; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 469


March 10, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
May 25, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Lincoln Loan Co. v. City of Portland, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 877 (Or., Sept. 6, 2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lincoln Loan Co. v. Portland, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 2118 (U.S., Feb. 20, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 0307-07891; CA A124756. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Linda L. Bergman, Judge.


Lincoln Loan Co. v. City of Portland, 337 Ore. 308, 99 P.3d 290, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 638 (2004)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner loan company filed a declaratory judgment action to set aside an order from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), reversing the money judgment the Multnomah County Circuit Court awarded respondent city, on the ground that the Court of Appeals did not lawfully exist, and to obtain a declaration that the 1995 judgment was valid and enforceable.


OVERVIEW: The loan company obtained a money judgment (1995 judgment) against the city. The city appealed, and the Court of Appeals reversed the judgment. After the final appellate judgment, the company filed a declaratory judgment action, seeking to set aside the judgment. The company claimed that the Court of Appeals "did not lawfully exist." It alleged that the 1910 initiative measure that amended Or. Const. art. VII violated the Oregon Constitution as it had been adopted improperly; therefore, the Court of Appeals had no jurisdiction and no authority to overturn the 1995 judgment. The trial court granted summary judgment to the city, holding that the company had filed its challenge to the 1910 initiative too late to comply with any applicable statutes of limitations and that it had no standing. The court affirmed, ruling that the company was barred under the doctrine of claim preclusion to collaterally attack the prior judgment. The court noted that the company asserted the same claim that it asserted in the earlier case, that it sought recovery from the same party, and that the "grounds or theories" that the company asserted here could have been raised and litigated in the prior case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court in favor of the city.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 02CR0019. On automatic and direct review of judgments of conviction and sentences of death imposed by the Curry County Circuit Court. Richard K. Mickelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of conviction and sentences of death are affirmed. The case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Curry County Circuit Court (Oregon), which convicted defendant of two counts of aggravated murder and sentenced him to death under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.012(1).


OVERVIEW: After defendant beat his ex-girlfriend, he went to a friend's (victim) house. Defendant claimed that he and the victim struggled with defendant's gun when the victim tried to call police, and it went off. Defendant then took several items from the house. The court held: (1) photographs of the ex-girlfriend's injuries were properly admitted because they were relevant and not unfairly prejudicial under Or. Evid. OUTCOME: The court remanded for entry of a corrected judgment reflecting defendant's guilty of the charge of aggravated murder based upon alternative aggravating factors, and imposing one sentence of death. The court otherwise affirmed the judgments of conviction and the sentences of death.

Outdoor Media Dimensions, Inc. v. DOT

September 10, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
March 23, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Reconsideration denied by Outdoor Media Dimensions, Inc. v. Dep't of Transp., 2006 Ore. LEXIS 669 (Or., June 20, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: ODOT 88691, 89838, 90587, 90588, 90589, 91564; ODOT 79817, 85185; DMV 66981, 68677, 70642, 74573, 74889; DMV 58118; DMV 61907, 63047; CA A116814; CA A113875 (control), A114027; CA A106450; CA A102328; CA A100658 (control), A100659. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from orders of the Driver and Motor Vehicle Services Branch and the Department of Transportation. Outdoor Media Dimensions, Inc. v. ODOT, 187 Ore. nullnull
Outdoor Media Dimensions v. Driver & Motor Vehicle Servs. Branch (DMV), 184 Ore. App. 501, 56 P.3d 522, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1647 (2002)
Outdoor Media Dimensions v. Driver & Motor Vehicle Servs. Branch (DMV), 184 Ore. App. 502, 56 P.3d 935, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1648 (2002)
Outdoor Media Dimensions, Inc. v. DOT, 185 Ore. App. 161, 57 P.3d 970, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1877 (2002)
Outdoor Media Dimensions v. DOT, 187 Ore. App. 503, 68 P.3d 274, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 634 (2003)
Outdoor Media Dimensions v. Driver & Motor Vehicle Servs. Branch (DMV), 184 Ore. App. 495, 56 P.3d 935, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1636 (2002)



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the Court of Appeals and the orders of the Driver and Motor Vehicle Services Branch and the Department of Transportation are reversed. Case numbers S50003, S50007, and S50044 are remanded to the Driver and Motor Vehicle Services Branch for further proceedings, and case numbers S49978 and S50458 are remanded to the Department of Transportation [***2] for further proceedings. Riggs, J., concurred in part and dissented in part and filed an opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sign company appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirming the trial court's ruling on decisions of respondents, the department of transportation and the driver and motor vehicles branch (State), which cited the company for displaying one or more of its billboards without a permit in violation of the Oregon Motorist Information Act (OMIA), Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 377.700 to 377.840 and 377.992 (1999).


OVERVIEW: The company asserted that various provisions of the OMIA, on their face, violated the federal and state constitutions. The court held that because the OMIA's permit and fee requirements focused on covering the cost of a content-neutral permit scheme and were implemented in a nonarbitrary manner, the requirements did not constitute prior restraints on expression in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 8. Also, Or. Rev. Stat. § 377.729 expressly tied the fee to the cost of the regulatory program. Thus, the court could not conclude that the fee requirement was an impermissible restriction on speech. Furthermore, Or. Const. art. I, § 8 did not prohibit reasonable time, place, and manner regulation of speech imposed for reasons apart from the message of the speech. However, on-premises signs, which did not require a permit, and off-premises signs, which did require a permit, could pose the same risk to safety and have the same adverse effect on scenic beauty. Therefore, the legislature's decision to limit one of those types of expression more stringently than the other because of its content was an impermissible restriction on the "subject" of expression under art. I, § 8.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the agency orders and remanded to the respective agencies.

Lombardo v. Warner


SC S51989


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


340 Ore. 264; 132 P.3d 22; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 222


June 22, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
March 23, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CV 98-03001-MRH; US Ninth Circuit No. 02-35269. On certified questions from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. Order dated December 3, 2004; certification accepted January 25, 2005. Honorable Mary M. Schroeder, Chief Judge, and Honorable Warren J. Ferguson, Honorable Andrew J. Kleinfeld, Honorable A. Wallace Tashima, Honorable Sidney R. Thomas, Honorable M. Margaret McKeown, Honorable Kim McClane Wardlaw, Honorable Raymond C. Fisher, Honorable Marsha S. Berzon, Honorable Richard R. Clifton, and Honorable Jay S. Bybee, United States Ninth Circuit Court Judges.
Lombardo v. Warner, 391 F.3d 1008, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 24915 (9th Cir. Or., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Certified questions answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicant filed an action against defendant Oregon Department of Transportation, which arose from a dispute under the Oregon Motorist Information Act (OMIA), Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 377.700 to 377.840. The case was before the court on certified questions from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit.


OVERVIEW: The applicant sought to display a large sign. He challenged the variance provision of Or. Rev. Stat. § 377.735(2), arguing that it violated the First Amendment because it allowed unbridled discretion to grant or deny a variance. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit certified questions to the court regarding the OMIA. In answering the questions, the court determined that good cause shown in the context of § 377.735(2) meant that the party seeking the variance was required to demonstrate a sufficient reason to permit it to display a temporary sign that did not meet the requirements of § 377.735(1)(b). The interpretation of the phrase "for good cause shown" was not a phrase entirely within the discretion of the Department. The discretion was limited by Or. Admin. R. 734-060-0175(2), and it was not permitted to disregard this rule as long as it was in effect. Moreover, the Department's discretion was limited by the state and federal constitutions. Finally, Oregon law required the Department to respond to a variance request within a reasonable time, and judicial review was available if the Department failed to act within that time.


OUTCOME: The certified questions were answered.

Allen v. County of Jackson County


SC S51180


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


340 Ore. 146; 129 P.3d 694; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 148


November 4, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
February 24, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 97-0009-LI(8); CA A115689. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Jackson County Circuit Court, Roxanne Osborne, Judge. 191 Ore. App. 185, 82 P.3d 628 (2003).


Allen v. County of Jackson County, 191 Ore. App. 185, 82 P.3d 628, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1725 (2003)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county employees challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals, Oregon, affirming a decision by the circuit court, awarding the employee's 0.3 percent damages in their breach of contract claim and Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 652 statutory wage claim, filed against defendant county.


OVERVIEW: The underlying litigation resulted from a change in state law regarding who must pay employee pension contributions amounting to 6 percent of salary, a change later held unconstitutional. Before that change was declared unconstitutional, the county responded to it with a payroll scheme that deducted 6 percent from certain employees' pay, but also simultaneously paid the same employees a 5.7 percent pay increase. Because the law requiring the 6 percent deduction was held unconstitutional, the previously authorized 6 percent deductions became unlawful retrospectively. The employees whose paychecks were affected argued that they were entitled to the 6 percent of their salary that the county unlawfully deducted from their paychecks. The circuit court awarded 0.3 percent damages, and the court of appeals affirmed. The reviewing court held that the "actual damages" under Or. Rev. Stat. § 652.615 was 0.3 percent, not 6 percent. Of the 6 percent deducted for retirement contributions, the county returned 5.7 percent simultaneously, in every paycheck, so only the net amount, 0.3 percent, ever was "deducted."


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals and the judgment of the circuit court were affirmed.


 

112 of 770 DOCUMENTS



Macpherson v. Dep't of Admin. Servs.


SC S52875


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


340 Ore. 117; 130 P.3d 308; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 104


January 10, 2006, Argued and Submitted  
February 21, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC No. 05C10444. On direct review of the General Judgment and Order dated October 24, 2005, filed in Marion County Circuit Court, Mary Mertens James, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded for entry of judgment in favor of defendants and intervenors.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, counties and state officials, appealed a judgment of the Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon) that was entered in favor of respondent property owners and declared that Ballot Measure 37 (2004), codified at Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.352, was unconstitutional. Measure 37 required state and local governments to compensate property owners for the reduction in the fair market value of their property resulting from land use regulations.


OVERVIEW: In lieu of providing just compensation, Measure 37, Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.352(8), allowed the governing body that enacted a land use regulation to refrain from applying the regulation. The trial court declared that Measure 37 was invalid. On appeal, the court held that the trial court erred in declaring that Measure 37 was unconstitutional because Measure 37 did not violate separation of powers constraints and did not limit the plenary power of the legislature or the people to enact legislation as provided in Or. Const. art. IV, § 1. Also, Measure 37 did violate the equal privileges and immunities guarantee of Or. Const. art. I, § 20 by limiting its provisions to property owners that had acquired their land prior to enactment of the land use regulations that they wished to avoid. The court concluded that the property owners failed to meet their burden to show that Measure 37 bore no reasonable relation to a legitimate state interest. The court found that the people, in exercising their initiative power, were free to enact Measure 37 in furtherance of policy objectives such as compensating landowners for a diminution in property value resulting from land use regulations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment that held ballot Measure 37 was unconstitutional and remanded the case for entry of judgment in favor of appellants.

Greenberg v. Myers


SC S52837


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


340 Ore. 65; 127 P.3d 1192; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 83


November 28, 2005, Submitted on the record  
February 9, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Greenberg v. Myers, 131 P.3d 195, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 457 (Or., Feb. 22, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition to review ballot title.



DISPOSITION: Ballot title referred to Attorney General for modification.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.085(2), petitioner electors asked the court to review respondent Attorney General's certified ballot title for a proposed initiative measure that the Oregon Secretary of State designated as Initiative Petition 51 (2006). The electors challenged the caption, the "yes" vote result statement, the "no" vote result statement, and the summary.


OVERVIEW: The proposed measure related to abortion notification requirements and a proposed enforcement scheme. The court agreed with the electors that the caption had to be modified. The court noted that it was undebatable that the new enforcement scheme in the proposed measure would enact major changes to Oregon law that likely would be significant to the voting public. By failing to give any hint of the new remedial scheme in the caption, the Attorney General understated the scope of the legal changes that the proposed measure would enact, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(2)(a). The court next determined that the Attorney General's "yes" vote result statement did not include any reference to the measure's proposed administrative discipline feature, which was necessary to describe accurately the enforcement scheme in the proposed measure. The court also determined that the "no" vote result statement was incomplete. It failed to disclose current Oregon law on parental involvement in abortion decisions for minor females younger than 15 years old. Finally, the court found that the summary required modification to reflect pertinent law concerning females younger than 15 years old.


OUTCOME: The court referred the ballot title to the Attorney General for modification.

Williams v. Philip Morris, Inc.


SC S51805


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


340 Ore. 35; 127 P.3d 1165; 2006 Ore. LEXIS 31; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P17,452


May 10, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
February 2, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Philip Morris USA v. Williams, 126 S. Ct. 2329, 164 L. Ed. 2d 838, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 4161 (U.S., 2006)
Vacated by, Remanded by Philip Morris USA v. Williams, 127 S. Ct. 1057, 166 L. Ed. 2d 940, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 1332 (U.S., 2007)
Adhered to, Remanded by Williams v. Philip Morris, Inc., 2008 Ore. LEXIS 5 (Or., Jan. 31, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 9705-03957; CA A106791. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Anna J. Brown, Judge. 182 Ore. App. 44, 48 P.3d 824 (2002), adh'd to on recons, 183 Ore. App. 192, 51 P.3d 670, rev den, 335 Ore. 142, 61 P.3d 938 (2002), vac'd and rem'd, 540 US 801, 124 S Ct 56, 157 L Ed 2d 12 (2003), on remand, 193 Ore. App. 527, 92 P.3d 126 (2004).


Williams v. Philip Morris, Inc., 193 Ore. App. 527, 92 P.3d 126, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 677 (2004)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tobacco company appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reinstated the trial court's award in respondent representative's action for negligence and fraud, asserting a causal connection between her husband's smoking habit and his death. The jury awarded respondent punitive damages of $79.5 million. The trial court reduced that amount.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals noted that the jury could have found that petitioner had little doubt that cigarette smoking was causally related to a number of diseases. Petitioner continued to assert that the hazard of cigarette smoking to health was uncertain when it knew that there was no controversy about that subject. The court affirmed, and held that evidence of similar conduct against other parties may be relevant to a punitive damage award. Thus the trial court properly refused to give petitioner's proposed instruction that the jury could not punish petitioner for the impact of its alleged misconduct on other persons. There could be no dispute that petitioner's conduct was extraordinarily reprehensible. Petitioner spread false information to suggest to the public that doubts remained about that issue. Knowing that it was putting smokers' lives at risk, it continued to do so for nearly half a century. The first Gore guidepost favored a very significant punitive damage award. The court concluded that the ratio guidepost considered only harm to the plaintiff. Under such outrageous circumstances, the court concluded that the jury's punitive damage award comported with due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals. The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded.

State v. Heilman


SC S51479


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 661; 125 P.3d 728; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 811

* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Frank L. Bearden, Judge. 193 Ore. App. 329, 92 P.3d 767 (2004).

DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon) applying a 20-year enhancement to his prison sentence as a dangerous offender under Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.725(1)(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of 21 crimes arising from a criminal episode that began when he broke into his ex-wife's house, and ended when he attempted to take a store clerk hostage. Defendant waived his right to a jury trial and asserted that he was guilty except for insanity. The court rejected defendant's argument that the state should have submitted the dangerous-offender enhancement to a jury because he failed to preserve the argument for review by waiving his right to a jury trial. Defendant did not purport to qualify his waiver in any way; nor did his counsel make any kind of objection or assertion that defendant wanted a jury to determine the facts for purposes of sentencing. The court also rejected defendant's argument that under Apprendi the state was required to plead the elements of the dangerous-offender enhancement in the indictment because Apprendi required no such thing.


OUTCOME: The decision applying a 20-year enhancement to defendant's sentence was affirmed.

State v. Upton


SC S52316


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 673; 125 P.3d 713; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 814


September 9, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
December 30, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of mandamus granted State v. Upton, 340 Ore. 107, 129 P.3d 185, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 177 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC No. 04-419.
State v. Upton, 338 Ore. 585, 114 P.3d 505, 2005 Ore. LEXIS 282 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ of mandamus to issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State filed an original proceeding in mandamus, which sought a determination of whether a trial court had the authority under Oregon law to submit certain sentence enhancement factors to a jury for factual determination under the Sixth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's indictment alleged several aggravating factors. The trial court refused to submit the aggravating factors regarding defendant's past crimes and a vulnerable victim to the jury. The State then filed a petition for a writ of mandamus. In granting the writ, the court determined that the trial court erred by failing to submit the sentence enhancement factors to the jury. First, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 136.130, 136.320 authorized the trial court to submit all questions of fact to the jury that a criminal defendant was entitled to have the jury decide. Next, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 137.669, 137.671(1), and Or. Admin. R. 213-008-0002 did not place any limitation on the use of the jury to make the factual findings necessary to support the imposition of an enhanced sentence. The court rejected defendant's argument that Or. Senate Bill 528, 2005 Or. Laws ch. 463, violated either Or. Const. art. I, § 21 or U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 because there was no disadvantageous change. The Fifth Amendment was not violated since defendant was placed on notice through the indictment. Finally, despite Or. Rev. Stat. § 132.540(2), the indictment was permitted to make reference to the prior convictions.


OUTCOME: The peremptory writ of mandamus was issued.

Am. Trucking Ass'ns v. DOT


SC S51622, S51623


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 554; 124 P.3d 1210; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 726


May 10, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
December 15, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Am. Trucking Ass'n v. Or Dep't of Transp., 126 S. Ct. 2960, 165 L. Ed. 2d 950, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 4920 (U.S., June 26, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC No. 00C16242; CA A117694. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Claudia Burton, Judge. 193 Ore. App. 185, 90 P.3d 15 (2004).


Am. Trucking Ass'ns v. State, 193 Ore. App. 185, 90 P.3d 15, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 539 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the Oregon Department of Transportation, its director, and a transportation association, filed a petition for review of a decision of the court of appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a trial court judgment as to flat-fee alternatives for farm-use trucks but reversed regarding the tax alternatives for non-farm-use trucks. The trial court had dismissed the action filed by plaintiffs, a trucking association and trucking companies.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs had sought a judgment declaring that the provisions of Or. Rev. Stat. § 825.480(1), (4), and (5), that offered a flat-fee tax option to certain non-farm-use carriers, as well as similar provisions for farm-use trucks, violated the Commerce Clause. The court of appeals concluded that the flat-fee alternatives for non-farm-use carriers violated the Commerce Clause by creating a fee structure that had a discriminatory effect on trucks engaged in interstate commerce. On review, the court determined that Complete Auto Transit's four-factor test was the appropriate analytical format for reviewing the issues presented. It then analyzed whether the flat-fee option at issue was both malapportioned and discriminatory. Applying the internal consistency test, the court discerned no apportionment problem under the Complete Auto Transit analysis. The flat-fee option was aimed at operations that took place primarily in Oregon and did not tax an interstate truck's entry into Oregon. Noting that the flat-fee option did not facially discriminate against interstate commerce, the court rejected plaintiffs' hypothetical projections as adequate evidence of discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals pertaining to non-farm-use carriers and affirmed the remainder of that decision. The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court.

City of Nyssa v. Dufloth


SC S49963


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 330; 121 P.3d 639; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 560


November 3, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
September 29, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by City of Nyssa v. Dufloth, 2005 Ore. LEXIS 803 (Or., Dec. 20, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC A00080112, A00080111; CA A113180 (control), A113181. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Malheur County Circuit Court, Rodney W. Miller, Judge. 184 Ore. App. 631, 57 P.3d 161 (2002).


City of Nyssa v. Dufloth, 184 Ore. App. 631, 57 P.3d 161, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1716 (2002)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, owners of a nude dancing club, were convicted of violating a local ordinance that required, among other things, that entertainers at such clubs remain at least four feet away from the patrons. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed, and defendants sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals found that, even if the ordinance were directed at expression, it was not unconstitutional because nude dancing was not protected under Or. Const. art. I, § 8. In reversing, the court found that the four-foot ordinance violated art. I, § 8. By its terms, the ordinance applied only to one disfavored type of communication (nude performances) in one disfavored type of establishment (one that regularly featured that type of entertainment). Thus, Nyssa, Or., City Code § 5.10.130(17), restrained free expression. The court disagreed with the conclusion that the rule fell within a well-established historical exception for laws regulating public sexual conduct. A historical exception could not be established with respect to laws directed at regulating the portrayal of sexual matters merely by showing that similar regulations were longstanding at the time of the adoption of Or. Const. art. I, § 8, if those historical criminal prohibitions were directed exclusively at protecting the hearer or the viewer from a disfavored message. The ordinance was unconstitutional on its face, and defendants' convictions had to be reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the court of appeals and the circuit court and remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Lawson v. Hoke


SC S51044


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 253; 119 P.3d 210; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 516


September 10, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
September 9, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Lawson v. Hoke, 2005 Ore. LEXIS 623 (Or., Oct. 20, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 0101-00766; CA A117388. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Robert W. Redding, Senior Judge. 190 Ore. App. 91, 77 P.3d 1160 (2003).


Lawson v. Hoke, 190 Ore. App. 91, 77 P.3d 1160, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1363 (2003)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an uninsured motorist, sued defendant motorist for both economic and noneconomic injuries arising from an accident in which defendant was at fault. The circuit court denied defendant's motion for partial summary judgment as to noneconomic damages and, after a bench trial, awarded noneconomic damages. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed that award under Or. Rev. Stat. § 31.715. Plaintiff sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Or. Rev. Stat. § 31.715 precluded uninsured drivers from recovering noneconomic damages for injuries sustained in an action arising out of the operation of a motor vehicle. Plaintiff argued that the right to recover such damages was a fundamental right and that § 31.715 violated Or. Const. art. I, § 10. The court disagreed, finding that no absolute common-law right that existed when the Oregon Constitution was drafted in 1857 would have guaranteed a remedy for her injuries to a person in plaintiff's position as violator of the law requiring drivers to have insurance. The court also found that no other source of law, including Or. Const. art. I, § 17, authorized the claim for noneconomic damages. Therefore, the legislature's choice to preclude uninsured drivers from recovering noneconomic damages also did not implicate the right to a jury trial under art. I, § 17, which did not create substantive claims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

Richards v. Bd. of Parole


SC S51773


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 176; 118 P.3d 261; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 511


June 21, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
August 18, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by, Petition granted by Richards v. Bd. of Parole, 2007 Ore. LEXIS 961 (Or., Oct. 4, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CA A124095. On review from the Court of Appeals.* 


* Appeal from Court of Appeals Order of Dismissal dated September 9, 2004. Mary J. Diets, Chief Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought review of an order from the Oregon Court of Appeals, which dismissed for lack of jurisdiction his challenge to an order from respondent, the Oregon State Board of Parole and Post-Prison Supervision, changing his projected release date to one year but requiring that he undergo a psychological evaluation before release.


OVERVIEW: The inmate sought administrative review of an order that delayed his release date for two years and required that he undergo a psychological evaluation before release. In response, the Board entered a new order that changed his projected release date to one year but retained the requirement that he undergo an evaluation. The inmate sought review in the lower court. The Board moved to dismiss, arguing that its order did not adversely affect or aggrieve the inmate in the manner necessary to establish appellate jurisdiction. The lower court dismissed the petition for lack of jurisdiction. On appeal, the court found that whatever the merits of the inmate's argument, he did not receive all of the relief that he had sought when the Board entered the second order. The second order placed the inmate under a disability that, he asserted, could not be imposed on him. Accordingly, the inmate was "adversely affected or aggrieved" by the second order for purposes of Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.335(1)(a). The inmate's legal theory might not succeed, but he established that he was entitled to have it addressed either directly on the merits or, if the doctrine applied, as a matter of issue preclusion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

122 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Harris


SC S51600


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 157; 118 P.3d 236; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 512


January 12, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
August 18, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC No. C011903CR; CA A11718. On review from the Court of Appeals.* 


* Appeal from Washington County Circuit Court, Alan C. Bonebrake, Judge. 191 Ore. App. 305, 82 P.3d 653 (2003).


State v. Harris, 192 Ore. App. 602, 89 P.3d 96, 2004 Ore. App. LEXIS 319 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the trial court's sentencing of defendant upon his guilty plea to first-degree theft, first-degree burglary, and identity theft. The trial court relied upon defendant's prior juvenile adjudication to increase his criminal history score and to impose an upward departure sentence.


OVERVIEW: No sentencing stipulations had been made as part of defendant's plea agreement. Defendant argued that because his prior juvenile adjudication had lacked a jury trial, the fact of his adjudication could not be used to increase his sentence and remain consonant with Apprendi. The court held that it was undisputed that defendant's prior juvenile adjudication qualified as "any fact" under Apprendi and Blakely, and that the State did not present evidence of the juvenile adjudication to a jury, but used the adjudication specifically to lengthen his prison sentence beyond the maximum that he otherwise could have received. Defendant expressly objected to the use of the juvenile adjudication at his sentencing hearing. Therefore, defendant's acknowledgment of a prior juvenile adjudication in filling out his guilty plea did not amount to either an admission or a knowing waiver of his jury trial right for sentencing purposes. Because the prior adjudication's existence was not proved to a trier of fact or admitted by defendant for sentencing purposes, the trial court's use of the juvenile adjudication as an enhancement factor violated the Sixth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals. The conviction was affirmed and the sentence was vacated.

Coast Range Conifers, LLC v. State


SC S51342


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 136; 117 P.3d 990; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 466; 61 ERC (BNA) 1133


January 7, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
August 11, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 011423; A117769. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Lincoln County Circuit Court, Robert J. Huckleberry, Judge. 189 Ore. App. 531, 76 P.3d 1148 (2003).


Coast Range Conifers, LLC v. State, 189 Ore. App. 531, 76 P.3d 1148, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1317 (2003)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner on review Oregon State Board of Forestry appealed the decision from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the decision from the circuit court that had determined that state regulations that prohibited respondent on review property owner from logging on a parcel of its property did not amount to a taking in violation Or. Const. art. I, § 18 or U.S. Const. amend. V.


OVERVIEW: The owner was a company that bought a 40-acre tract of property, which it intended to log; however, bald eagles made a nest in the tract. Pursuant to Or. Admin. R. 629-605-0170(1)(d), the owner had to submit a written plan to the State Forester before engaging in any logging activities that might damage the nest site. The owner received approval to log all but nine acres surrounding the nest site. After the nesting season, the owner sought but was denied approval to log the remaining nine acres. It filed an unsuccessful suit, but the court of appeals reversed. On appeal, the court agreed with the trial court, finding that in determining whether the regulation deprived the owner of all economically beneficial use of the property and thus took the property, the proper focus was the entire 40-acre parcel, not the nine acre parcel affected by the regulation. The court then held that because the owner's property had an economically viable use, the regulation did not effect a taking under Or. Const. art. I, § 18. Furthermore, under the whole parcel rule, the owner's U.S. Const. amend. V claim based on the holding in Lucas failed because the owner was able to log 31 acres of its property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision from the court of appeals and affirmed the judgment of the circuit court.

State v. Hall


SC S49825


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


339 Ore. 7; 115 P.3d 908; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 374


November 4, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
July 15, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
CC 9701546CR; CA A109813. On review from the Court of Appeals.* 


* Appeal from Klamath County Circuit Court, Richard Rambo, Judge. 183 Ore. App 48, 50 P.3d 1258 (2002).


State v. Hall, 183 Ore. App. 48, 50 P.3d 1258, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1190 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of possession of amphetamine after the trial court denied his motion to suppress. The Oregon Court of Appeals reversed, holding that Or. Const. art. I, § 9 required exclusion of the challenged evidence because the police officer had stopped defendant unlawfully and then had "exploited" that unlawful stop to obtain defendant's consent to search. The State sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped by an officer who noticed defendant twice glance towards him and then quickly look away, and after he had passed defendant, watched defendant turn around to look at him several more times. The officer stopped by defendant's car and asked defendant to approach him and provide him with identification. The officer then ran a warrant check. While waiting for the results of the warrant check, the officer asked defendant if he was carrying any illegal drugs or weapons. The officer also asked for, and received, defendant's consent to search him. The search revealed a vial of amphetamines. The state supreme court affirmed the court of appeals and held that (1) the stop of defendant was unlawful as the officer did not have a reasonable suspicion that defendant had committed a crime under Or. Rev. Stat. § 131.615(1) (1995) and (2) Or. Const. art. I, § 9 required the exclusion of the drugs found after the search as defendant's consent was not spontaneous but, instead, was made only in response to the officer's request that defendant allow a search while waiting for the results of the warrant check, which in conjunction with the unlawful seizure vitiated his consent.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals and reversed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Shaw


SC S51416


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


338 Ore. 586; 113 P.3d 898; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 340


October 27, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
June 16, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 010331742. On appeal from the Multnomah County Circuit Court. David Gernant, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a decision of the Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon) dismissing with prejudice an indictment charging defendant with aggravated murder under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095(2)(d) after the State refused to proceed with the prosecution because it intended to appeal a pretrial exclusionary ruling that the trial court had made under Or. Evid. Code 403.


OVERVIEW: Before defendant's aggravated murder trial, the trial court ruled that the State could not prove one of defendant's other crimes through the testimony of the surviving victim because the testimony would be prejudicial under § 403. The court reversed the dismissal, holding that in reviewing the legislative history, Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.060(2) authorized the State's appeal to the court. As to the original merits, the court noted that when a prosecutor is not ready for trial, and a defendant moves for dismissal, then Or. Rev. Stat. § 136.120 first requires the trial court to determine whether the prosecutor has shown "any sufficient cause" for postponing the defendant's trial. Because the State had the right to appeal the ruling, the trial court did not have authority under § 136.120 to dismiss the indictment. However, the trial court did not err by applying Or. Evid. Code 403 to decide the admissibility of testimony at issue in the guilt phase of defendant's trial, but the trial court abused its discretion under in choosing instead to exclude the testimony altogether. Finally, the court rejected defendant's challenge to the relevance of the other crimes evidence.


OUTCOME: The order of the circuit court dismissing the indictment and the judgment of acquittal were reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings. The cross-appeal was dismissed.

McFadden v. Dryvit Sys.


SC S51901


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


338 Ore. 528; 112 P.3d 1191; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 273; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P17,429


March 7, 2005, Argued and Submitted  
May 26, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by McFadden v. Dryvit Sys., 2005 Ore. LEXIS 436 (Or., July 19, 2005)
Summary judgment granted by, Dismissed by McFadden v. Dryvit Sys., 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 14726 (D. Or., Mar. 13, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] USDC CV-04-103-HA; On certified question from United States District Court for the District of Oregon Order dated October 29, 2004; certification accepted November 23, 2004. Honorable Ancer L. Haggerty, Chief Judge, United States District Court for the District of Oregon.
McFadden v. Dryvit Sys., Inc., 337 Ore. 617, 103 P.3d 639, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 795 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners filed a products liability suit against defendant siding manufacturing company. The U.S. District Court for the District or Oregon certified a question for the court that the court revised to read: whether § 2 of the 2003 amendments to Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.905, 2003 Or. Laws 768, § 2, violated the separation of powers clause in Or. Const. art. VII, § 1 or Or. Const. art. III, § 1.


OVERVIEW: The homeowners sought to recover damages allegedly resulting from improper installation of an exterior stucco siding system. In 2001, they filed a lawsuit against the siding manufacturer that was dismissed as time-barred under Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.905. The Oregon legislature amended § 30.905, effective January 1, 2004, to allow a products liability action to be commenced within two years after the date on which a plaintiff discovers or reasonably could have discovered the injury and revived any such cause of action that was dismissed as untimely prior to January 1, 2004. The homeowners then filed the instant suit. The court determined that in enacting the 2003 amendment to § 30.905, the legislature did not retroactively declare what the law had been; not did the amendment operate to annul or set aside a final judgment of a court on the merits; rather, it gave litigants a new right to file an action and in so doing, the legislature performed the legislative function of "declaring what the law shall be" with respect to newly filed claims. Accordingly, the court found there was no separation of powers violation and that the statute did not violate the Oregon constitution.


OUTCOME: In enacting the 2003 amendment to Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.905 and thereby reviving certain product liability actions for which a court had entered a final judgment of dismissal before the effective date of the amendment, the legislature did not violate the separation of powers provisions in Or. Const. art. VII, § 1 or Or. Const. art. III, § 1.
