Li v. State


SC S51612


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


338 Ore. 376; 110 P.3d 91; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 144


December 15, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
April 14, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 0403-03057. CA A124877. On certified appeal from the Court of Appeals. * 


* On appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Frank L. Bearden, Judge.


Li v. State, 337 Ore. 249, 95 P.3d 730, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 490 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action for declaratory and injunctive relief, defendant State appealed a circuit court judgment that Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 106 violated Or. Const. art. I, § 20, by denying certain benefits to same-sex couples that otherwise were available to married opposite-sex couples by virtue of their marriages. The Oregon Court of Appeals certified the appeal to the instant court pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 19.405(1).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were nine same-sex couples, an advocacy group, a civil liberties organization, and a county. The chair of the county board of commissioners had ordered the county's records management division to issue marriage licenses to same-sex couples who applied for such licenses from the county. Plaintiffs argued that the right to marry was a "privilege" under the Oregon Constitution and that Or. Const. art. I, § 20, prohibited using sexual orientation or gender as a basis to deny such a privilege. The court held that Oregon law placed the regulation of marriage exclusively within the province of the state's legislative power. It also held that the use of the word "policy" in Or. Ballot Measure 36 (2004), which amended the Oregon Constitution, was intended to signal a presently enforceable tenet of state constitutional law. Under the Oregon Constitution, marriage in Oregon was limited to opposite-sex couples. Thus, the claims of five of the same-sex couples that they were entitled as a matter of state law to obtain marriage licenses and to marry failed. The county did not have the authority to issue the licenses for the marriages in question. The licenses were void ab initio.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court with instructions to dismiss the action.

Ryan v. Palmateer


SC S51169

338 Ore. 278; 108 P.3d 1127; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 105

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 99C-18624; CA A118415. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Joseph C. Guimond, Judge. 190 Ore. App. 397, 79 P.3d 916 (2003).


Ryan v. Palmateer, 190 Ore. App. 397, 79 P.3d 916, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 1557 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed a decision of the Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon), which in a post-conviction case held that petitioner's rights had not been violated. Petitioner alleged that he received inadequate assistance of counsel under Ore. Const. art. I, § 11 and U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of sodomy, attempted rape, third-degree theft, menacing and witness tampering. In the post-conviction proceeding, he submitted an affidavit from the trial judge stating that trial counsel's performance was poor. One of the jurors, during voir dire, stated that she believed friends who stated they had been raped. The court held that trial counsel's failure to dismiss that juror did not prejudice petitioner because he questioned her at length and petitioner presented no evidence to show that the juror was actually biased. Also, counsel stated that petitioner refused to allow him to negotiate a plea agreement. Although trial counsel's conduct of some aspects fell below the required standard of skill, petitioner did not prove that any of the mistakes affected the result of the prosecution. Strunk v. Public Emples. Ret. Bd.


SC S50593 (Control); S50647; S50645; S50532; S50686; S50685 (Cases Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


338 Ore. 145; 108 P.3d 1058; 2005 Ore. LEXIS 104


July 30, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
March 8, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Consol. Pub. Emples. Ret. Sys. Litig., 2005 Ore. LEXIS 248 (Or., Apr. 26, 2005)
Costs and fees proceeding at, En banc Strunk v. Public Emples. Ret. Bd., 341 Ore. 175, 139 P.3d 956, 2006 Ore. LEXIS 710 (July 20, 2006)
Related proceeding at Petrillo v. Pub. Emples. Ret. Bd., 286 Ore. App. 200, 2017 Ore. App. LEXIS 752 (June 14, 2017)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Oregon Laws 2003, chapter 67, sections 5, 6, 7, and 8, as amended by Oregon Laws 2003, chapter 625, sections 10, 11, and 12, are declared void. The final sentence in Oregon Laws 2003, chapter 67, section 10(3), is declared void. In all other respects, petitioners' claims for relief are denied or dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, petitioners, current and former public employees who were members of the Public Employees Retirement System (PERS), filed an action against respondents, the State, its agencies, the Public Employees Retirement Board (PERB), and other public employers, seeking a declaration that provisions of Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 238 (2003) unconstitutionally impaired the PERS statutory contract and an injunction to enjoin its implementation.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners challenged the discontinuation of contributions into their regular accounts under Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.200(4), which amended Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.200 (2001); and claimed that the PERS contracts were impaired because the contributions into an Individual Account Program (IAP) instead of their regular accounts were no longer guaranteed annual crediting at no less than the assumed earnings rate as provided in Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.255 (2001); were no longer subject to the Money Match formula under Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.300 (2001); and would not be enhanced by annual COLAs at retirement as provided in Or. Rev. Stat. § 238.360. The court held that the use of IAPs did not impair the contract and that members had no contractual right to a credit of annual earnings in excess of the assumed earnings rate. However, the court held that 2003 Or. Laws 67, §§ 5 to 8, as amended, which eliminated the annual assumed earnings rate credit to regular accounts of Tier One members, substantially impaired obligations under the contract in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 21; therefore they were void. Also the court held that the COLA suspension provision in 2003 Or. Laws 67, § 10(3) was void.


OUTCOME: The court held that the provisions of the 2003 PERS legislation that eliminated the annual assumed earnings rate credit to PERS Tier One members' regular accounts and that temporarily suspended annual COLAs to the service retirement allowances of retired Tier One members were void and of no effect. The court rejected petitioners' challenges in all other respects.


 

132 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dilts


SC S49525


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


337 Ore. 645; 103 P.3d 95; 2004 Ore. LEXIS 827


September 7, 2004, Submitted  
December 16, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 99CR-0172; CA A106034; USSC 03-9412. On remand from the United States Supreme Court.*


* Dilts v. Oregon, 542 US 934, 124 S Ct 2906, 159 L Ed 2d 809 (2004).


State v. Dilts, 336 Ore. 158, 82 P.3d 593, 2003 Ore. LEXIS 911 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's appeal of his conviction and sentence for assault was before the court on remand from the United States Supreme Court. In the court's prior decision, it had affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence. The Supreme Court vacated the judgment and remanded the case for further consideration in light of its Blakely decision.


OVERVIEW: On remand, defendant argued that, under Blakely, the sentence that the trial court imposed and that the court had affirmed violated his federal jury trial and due process rights. The court held that the Supreme Court's Blakely decision compelled it to reverse its previous decision. The court noted that the trial court had authority to impose its upward departure of defendant's sentence only because it made additional findings of fact, which was precisely the procedure that the Supreme Court found did not comply with the Sixth Amendment's jury trial guarantee. The Supreme Court held that, under the Sixth Amendment, a defendant had a right to have additional facts that might increase his or her sentence beyond the otherwise applicable maximum sentence decided by a jury, rather than by a trial judge. Therefore, defendant's sentence was constitutionally impermissible and had to be vacated. The court saw no basis upon which to sever a part of the Oregon Felony Sentencing Guidelines as the State suggested. Because the court had not held that a part of the Guidelines was unconstitutional, it could identify no unconstitutional part of the Guidelines that might be appropriate to sever.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the court of appeals and the trial court and remanded the case to the trial court. If the parties agreed, the trial court could sentence defendant to a sentence not exceeding the upper limit of the presumptive sentence under the Guidelines. If they disagreed, their positions for alternative means of determining the proper sentence had to be presented to and decided by the trial court in the first instance.

 

133 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Kucera v. Bradbury


SC S51756


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


337 Ore. 384; 97 P.3d 1191; 2004 Ore. LEXIS 674


September 17, 2004, Submitted  
September 22, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application denied by Kucera v. Bradbury, 542 U.S. 963, 159 L. Ed. 2d 855, 125 S. Ct. 27, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 5010 (U.S., 2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Kucera v. Bradbury, 125 S. Ct. 2290, 161 L. Ed. 2d 1102, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 4307 (U.S., May 23, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 04C18259. Original proceeding in mandamus. *


* On petition for a writ of mandamus from an order of the Marion County Circuit Court, Paul Lipscomb, Judge.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator, the Oregon Secretary of State, sought a writ of mandamus requiring the judge of the Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon) to vacate his order that required the Secretary to certify the nomination of an individual as an independent candidate on Oregon's November 2, 2004, general election ballot.


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether the Secretary was authorized to decline to count elector signatures on signature sheets that he found did not comply with applicable legal requirements in determining whether sufficient valid elector signatures supported the certificate of nomination. The court held that the Secretary was entitled to relief from the order. The Secretary determined, in the lawful exercise of delegated authority, that a circulator's violation of certification requirements deprived the affected signature sheet of the certification that Or. Rev. Stat. § 249.740(4) required. The Secretary of State's determination advanced the legislature's objective of deterring fraud in the nomination process. Or. Rev. Stat. § 247.005 did not purport to invalidate rules and procedures that the Secretary lawfully adopted pursuant to statutory command or delegated authority. The noncompliance by several counties with the Secretary's written instructions, issued under his express authority in Or. Rev. Stat. § 246.120, threatened a violation of the uniform application of his requirements for circulator certification. The Secretary's disqualification of signature sheets was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court directed that a peremptory writ of mandamus issue requiring the trial court to withdraw the order.

Yancy v. Shatzer


SC S50280


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


337 Ore. 345; 97 P.3d 1161; 2004 Ore. LEXIS 673


May 6, 2004, Argued and Submitted  
September 16, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 0008-08313; CA A114776. On review from the Court of Appeals.* 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Ellen F. Rosenblum, Judge. 185 Ore. App. 740, 60 P.3d 1156 (2003).


Yancy v. Shatzer, 185 Ore. App. 704, 60 P.3d 1156, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 31 (2003)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an individual who had been excluded from public parks in respondent City for a period of 30 days after he was found in possession of marijuana, sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which concluded that the case was moot because the period of exclusion had expired and ordered the circuit court to vacate its judgment and dismiss the matter as moot.


OVERVIEW: The court granted review to consider whether Oregon courts had the power to consider disputes that were capable of repetition and yet evaded review because they became moot at some point in the proceedings. In affirming, the court concluded that the judicial power under Or. Const. art. VII, § 1 did not include the authority to adjudicate cases in which there was no existing controversy. The court observed that the state constitution separated the power and functions of the departments of government pursuant to Or. Const. art. III, § 1 and vested in the courts only the judicial power. The court reviewed the history of Or. Const. art. VII, § 1 and determined that the framers of the state constitution would have understood the grant of judicial power in the restrained sense of an authority limited to the adjudication of an existing controversy. There was no exception for moot cases that were capable of repetition, yet evading review. The court overruled Perry v. Oregon Liquor Commission, 180 Or. 495, 498-99, 177 P.2d 406 (1947) and all cases that relied on it as authority for such an exception.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals and vacated the circuit court's decision.

O'Donnell-Lamont & Lamont


SC S50551


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


337 Ore. 86; 91 P.3d 721; 2004 Ore. LEXIS 364

June 10, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by O'Donnell-Lamont & Lamont, 2004 Ore. LEXIS 519 (Or., July 27, 2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lamont v. O'Donnell, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 454 (U.S., Jan. 10, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] C98 1284 DR; CA A112960. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Washington County Circuit Court, Steven L. Price, Judge. 184 Ore. App. 249, 56 P.3d 929 (2002), modified and adhered to as modified by an equally divided court, 187 Ore. App. 14, 67 P.3d 939 (2003).


O'Donnell-Lamont & Lamont, 187 Ore. App. 14, 67 P.3d 939, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 388 (2003)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed, and the judgment of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, grandparents, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which found in favor of respondent father and reversed the trial court's award of custody of the father's two children to the grandparents.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court allowed review of the child custody proceeding to consider the appropriate application of the changes that the legislature made to Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.119 in 2001. The trial court awarded custody of the two children to the grandparents, but the appellate court reversed. The supreme court reversed the decision of the appellate court, and affirmed the trial court's judgment. The 2001 amendments to § 109.119 applied because those amendments were applicable to all custody proceedings filed before, on, or after July 31, 2001. Further, considering all of the evidence, the grandparents rebutted, by a preponderance of the evidence, the presumption that the father acted in the best interest of the children. The supreme court found facts that supported four of the factors listed in § 109.119 that could be considered in determining whether the presumption was rebutted, including the grandparents' role as the primary caretakers to the children after the mother's death; the father's denial of contact between the children and grandparents by moving the children to another state; and the risk to the children that granting custody to the father would be detrimental to them.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the decision of the appellate court and affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Amaya


SC S49344

336 Ore. 616; 89 P.3d 1163; 2004 Ore. LEXIS 290

OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in a van that was stopped by a police officer. When the officer asked defendant what she had in her bag, she responded that she had a gun in the bag. State v. Ausmus


SC S49207 (Control), S49364)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


336 Ore. 493; 85 P.3d 864; 2004 Ore. LEXIS 138


March 5, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
March 11, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC Nos. 9901-40130, 9812-51296, 9901-40371, 9901-40129, 9901-40128, 9901-40231, 9905-13654, 9901-40232, 9901-40126, 9812-51298, 9901-40234; CA A107691 (Control), A107692, A107693, A107694, A107695, A107696, A107697, A107698, A107699, A107700, A107701. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeals from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Michael H. Marcus, Judge. 178 Ore. App. 321, 37 P.3d 1024 (2001).


State v. Ausmus, 178 Ore. App. 321, 37 P.3d 1024, 2001 Ore. App. LEXIS 1883 (2001).



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals' decision reversed; circuit court judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Several defendants were charged with disorderly conduct in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025(1)(e). Each defendant demurred to the accusatory instrument and raised constitutional challenges to the statute. The trial court sustained the demurrers, concluding that § 166.025(1)(e) was unconstitutionally vague. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed. The court allowed review of the consolidated cases.


OVERVIEW: Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025(1)(e), a person committed one variation of the crime of disorderly conduct when the person: (1) with the intent to cause, or by recklessly creating a risk of causing, public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm; (2) congregated with other persons in a public place; and (3) refused to comply with a lawful order of the police to disperse. The court concluded that, on its face, § 166.025(1)(e) was unconstitutionally overbroad because it restrained conduct that Or. Const. art. I, §§ 8 and 26 protected. The court stated that the difficulty with Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025(1)(e) was that a person ordered to disperse violated the statute regardless of whether any harm resulted from the refusal to disperse, the continued congregation with others, or the proscribed mental state. Thus, the statute applied to an individual who, in response to an order to disperse, abandoned whatever activity in which they were engaged that made the order lawful in the first place, but continued peaceably to congregate with others, with the intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm or recklessly created the risk of causing public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower appellate court and affirmed the judgments of the trial court.

State v. Dahl


SC S50053


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


336 Ore. 481; 87 P.3d 650; 2004 Ore. LEXIS 136


November 4, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
March 4, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC PR106249; CA A112549. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Steven R. Evans, Judge Pro Tempore. 185 Ore. App. 149, 57 P.3d 965 (2002).


State v. Dahl, 185 Ore. App. 149, 57 P.3d 965, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 1876 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed a judgment from the circuit court finding that defendant had committed a traffic violation. The court allowed review to consider the question of whether the State could rely on a statutory presumption in Or. Rev. Stat. § 810.439(1)(b) to prove a traffic violation.


OVERVIEW: A police officer using "photo radar" determined that a car registered to defendant had exceeded the speed limit. Pursuant to § 810.439(1), the State mailed a citation to defendant alleging that she had committed a speeding violation and defendant asked for a hearing. At the hearing, the State relied on the presumption in § 810.439(1)(b) to prove that defendant was the driver. Defendant moved to dismiss the case on the ground that due process prevented the State from relying on the presumption to prove an element of its case. The circuit court rejected defendant's constitutional challenges and found that defendant had committed the violation. The lower appellate court affirmed the judgment. On further appeal, the court held that the State validly relied on the presumption in § 810.439(1)(b). The court stated that, when the legislature referred to a "rebuttable presumption" in § 810.439(1)(b), it intended to refer to the procedural device described in Or. Evid. Code 308. Additionally, the presumption did not violate due process, as the legislature's determination that the registered owner was driving his or her car was not so unreasonable as to be a purely arbitrary mandate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower appellate court and the judgment of the circuit court.

DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; case remanded to circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court undertook automatic and direct review of a sentence of death imposed by the Deschutes County Circuit Court (Oregon), which, in a jury trial, convicted defendant of aggravated murder.

The prosecution conceded that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury as to life imprisonment sentencing options pursuant to former Or. Rev. I, § 42. The Ex Post Facto Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 was not implicated because the minimum quantum of evidence required to obtain a sentence of death was not reduced. Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.150(1) and the Eighth Amendment, alibi evidence was admissible in mitigation.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence of death and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner objectors filed an action against respondent Oregon Attorney General that challenged Or. Initiative Petition 40 (2004). Specifically, the objectors argued that the initiative did not substantially comply with Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(5).


OVERVIEW: An initiative sought to change Or. Rev. Stat. § 677.097 to require physicians to disclose certain information to patients in order to obtain the patient's informed consent. The objectors contended that the ballot title did not substantially comply with the requirements of Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(5). The "yes" or "no" statement contained in the measure was adequate. However, the court found that the summary improperly stated the law regarding informed consent under Or. Rev. Stat. § 677.097. Therefore, a modification was necessary.

State v. Perry


SC S48330


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


336 Ore. 49; 77 P.3d 313; 2003 Ore. LEXIS 717


May 7, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
October 9, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC 98-01-40848; CA A102784.


*  Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court. Ellen F. Rosenblum, Judge. 165 Ore. App. 342, 996 P2d 995 (2000).


State v. Perry, 165 Ore. App. 342, 996 P.2d 995, 2000 Ore. App. LEXIS 183 (2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the trial court decision. The trial court convicted defendant of unlawful possession of a firearm, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.250, and rejected his defense that, as an employee at a convenience store, his actions fell within the statutory "place of business" exception to the general license requirement for persons carrying concealed weapons.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was working alone and had responsibility for a convenience store when he was arrested for carrying a gun without a license. He argued that the "place of business" exception applied to him because a "person's place of business" was the place where that individual was employed. The court disagreed, finding that the "place of business" exception in § 166.250(2)(b) applied only to the owner of a business. The court reasoned that in 1925, the time of enactment, the phrase "place of business" referred to a location where a person conducted that person's own business, rather than to a location where a person was an employee. Further, the history demonstrated a legislative intent to carve out a limited and specific exception. The court agreed with the appeals court that if the general prohibition against possessing a concealed firearm without a license was constitutional, which defendant conceded, then § 166.250(2)(b), which allowed greater freedom to possess firearms, could not violate the constitutional right to bear arms. For purposes of Or. Const. art. I, § 20 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, drawing a distinction between business owners and employees was not irrational.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appeals court.

State v. Davis


SC S49523


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


336 Ore. 19; 77 P.3d 1111; 2003 Ore. LEXIS 650


March 11, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
October 2, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by State v. Davis, 2007 Ore. App. LEXIS 1802 (Or. Ct. App., Dec. 12, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 96051015; CA A106019. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Linn County Circuit Court, Daniel R. Murphy, Judge. 181 Ore. App. 467, 46 P.3d 229 (2002).


State v. Davis, 181 Ore. App. 467, 46 P.3d 229, 2002 Ore. App. LEXIS 710 (2002)



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the Court of Appeals and the circuit court are reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of murder. The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by excluding evidence of the victim's statements, which tended to indicate that the death was a suicide. The supreme court agreed. Although some of the evidence indicated that the victim was depressed and obsessed with defendant, the excluded statements were not merely cumulative of that evidence. The statements that defendant proffered were qualitatively different than the evidence that the jury heard. The excluded evidence went directly to the heart of defendant's factual theory of the case. Defendant sought to show that the victim was suicidal and obsessed with defendant and that, when defendant rejected her, she shot herself. The evidence at issue potentially was influential, because it tended to complete the picture of defendant's version of the events. Defendant was entitled to prove his theory of the case by presenting relevant, admissible evidence to the jury. The trial court's error in excluding the evidence could have affected the jury's determination whether there was reasonable doubt that defendant murdered the victim.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

146 of 770 DOCUMENTS


TVKO v. Howland


SC S48926


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


335 Ore. 527; 73 P.3d 905; 2003 Ore. LEXIS 505


May 7, 2003, Argued and Submitted  
July 24, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] OTC 4445. On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Senior Judge.


*  15 OTR 335 (2001), 15 OTR 402 (2001).


TVKO v. Howland, 15 Or. Tax 335, 2001 Ore. Tax LEXIS 218 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sporting event distributor sought review of the judgment of the Oregon Tax Court, which limited declaratory relief and denied a motion for attorney fees and costs in a case involving the constitutionality of a particular tax levied on certain pay-per-view telecasts that the distributor broadcast. Appellee state entities (State) consisted of various state entities that regulated professional boxing and wrestling in Oregon.


OVERVIEW: The distributor broadcast a pay-per-view boxing match from New York City and distributed the program to cable operators across the country. In accordance with Or. Rev. Stat. § 463.320(3), the State demanded the distributor pay a gross-receipts tax of $ 14,450 on the event. The Tax Court determined the tax violated the distributor's free speech rights. The Tax Court entered the State's proposed declaratory judgment. The appellate court concluded that the Tax Court did not commit legal error in entering the more limited judgment. It was unnecessary for the Tax Court to rule on the constitutionality of the statute as to broadcasts from events in Oregon. The distributor was not entitled to attorney fees or costs. The State had a right to rely on the opinion of the attorney general. The distributor and the assorted telecasters that it purported to represent were not "all citizens," neither could they pass for a significant part of the "public at large." The distributor also secured for itself a special and substantial pecuniary interest not shared with the general public.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Higgins v. DMV (In re Denial of Application for Custom Plates)


SC S48149


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


335 Ore. 481; 72 P.3d 628; 2003 Ore. LEXIS 394


March 14, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
July 3, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 60486; CA A96871; On review from the Court of Appeals.* 


*  Judicial Review from Administrative Law Judge, Teresa Hogan. 170 Ore. App. 542, 13 P3d 531 (2000).


Higgins v. Driver & Motor Vehicle Servs. Branch (DMV), 170 Ore. App. 542, 13 P.3d 531, 2000 Ore. App. LEXIS 1751 (2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a motorist, sought review from an order of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), where the majority opinion sustained a ruling of the Oregon Department of Transportation, Driver and Motor Vehicle Services Branch (DMV), that rejected petitioner's application for a license plate reading "WINE" or "VINO" on the theory that a customized registration plate conveyed communication by the state, not the vehicle owner.


OVERVIEW: The DMV determined that the requested license plates violated an administrative rule prohibiting references to alcoholic beverages on such plates. The DMV determined that petitioner's request was alcohol related. The DMV and a majority of a divided court of appeals rejected petitioner's contention that the rule violated his right to freedom of expression under Article I, section 8, of the Oregon Constitution and the First Amendment to the United States Constitution, because the administrative rule limited free speech on the basis of the content of that speech and did not confine its prohibition to some well-established historical exception to the state free speech guarantee. The court found the expression in this instance was that of the state in licensing the vehicle and not the protected speech of the driver of the vehicle, and, thus, distinguished cases where the state attempted to prohibit protected expressions.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was affirmed.

Martin v. City of Tigard


SC S46329


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


335 Ore. 444; 72 P.3d 619; 2003 Ore. LEXIS 348


September 11, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
June 12, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] OTC 4308. On review from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Judge.


* 14 OTR 517 (1999).


Martin v. City of Tigard, 14 Or. Tax 517, 1999 Ore. Tax LEXIS 6 (1999).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Tax Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers filed a claim in the Oregon Tax Court under Or. Rev. Stat. § 305.583(1) seeking a determination that respondent City of Tigard's charge was a tax under Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b rather than an assessment for local improvement. On opposing motions for summary judgment, the tax court granted summary judgment in the City's favor. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The City assessed the taxpayers for a road improvement project that benefited the taxpayers' property. The taxpayers claimed that the charge was a tax limited by Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b. The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of the City. The fact that the City allowed the payment of the charge over 10 years only if the taxpayers waived all irregularities or defects in the improvement proceedings did not preclude the charge from being a local improvement assessment because the conditional waiver added by voter initiative to Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b, was an option, not a right, and merely incorporated into the constitution a similar conditional waiver provision in Or. Rev. Stat. § 223.215(1)(a). The court also held that the inclusion of attorney fees and litigation costs associated with the project and with the underlying condemnation proceedings in the project did not preclude the charge from being a local improvement assessment. The court held that such costs were actual costs incurred in designing, constructing, and financing the project as contemplated under Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

149 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Swett v. Bradbury


SC S48116


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


335 Ore. 378; 67 P.3d 391; 2003 Ore. LEXIS 294



April 24, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 98-C-20484; CA A107552 (Control), A107799. On petition for attorney fees of respondents on review filed May 3, 2002.
Swett v. Bradbury, 333 Or. 597, 43 P.3d 1094, 2002 Ore. LEXIS 225 (2002).



DISPOSITION: Respondents awarded $ 21,992.50 as reasonable attorney fees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challengers of a ballot measure, who were successful challengers of the measure, which violated the separate-vote requirement of the Oregon Constitution, sought an award of attorney fees from defendant State and intervenor state officials for the challengers' efforts in securing that outcome. The challengers sought attorney fees totaling $ 21,992, which represented 80 hours of time devoted to their successful effort.


OVERVIEW: The challengers asserted they were entitled to an award of attorney fees under the rationale that a "court of equity" had inherent power to award attorney fees to a prevailing litigant in appropriate circumstances. The challengers asserted that such an award was justified because they successfully defended the integrity of the amendment and initiative process, an achievement that benefitted all Oregonians. The court held that none of the challengers were disqualified from receiving an award of attorney fees on the ground that the dominant characteristic of their case was that they were vindicating a private interest of their own, rather than vindicating an interest of the public generally. The case on the merits involved a declaratory judgment proceeding which had the power to grant equitable remedies where appropriate. An injunction against enforcement of the measure was not necessary as it was assumed that responsible State officials would honor the court's declaration without the necessity of an accompanying injunction. It was sufficient under the circumstances that the challengers invoked the court's declaratory judgment powers.


OUTCOME: The challengers petition against the State was allowed and the challengers were awarded $ 21,992 as reasonable attorney fees on review.

Newport Church of the Nazarene v. Hensley


SC S46621, S46769


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


335 Ore. 1; 56 P.3d 386; 2002 Ore. LEXIS 834; Unemployment Ins. Rep. (CCH) P8885


May 15, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
October 24, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] EAB No. 97-AB-2014; CA A99663. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


*  Appeal from Employment Appeals Board, 161 Ore. App. 12, 983 P.2d 1072 (1999).



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals affirmed in part and reversed in part; order of the Employment Appeals Board affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant youth pastor filed for unemployment compensation benefits after being fired by respondent church. The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed the Employment Appeals Board (board) award of unemployment compensation benefits and denial of attorney fees, but reversed the board's denial of interest. Both the church and the youth pastor sought review the court of appeals' decision.


OVERVIEW: The youth pastor was fired by the church. The youth pastor sought unemployment benefits and the church argued that under Or. Rev. Stat. § 657.072(1)(b), that ministers of churches were excluded from Oregon's unemployment compensation program. The appellate court held that in accordance prior case law and Or. Admin. R. 471-031-0090 (1996), Or. Rev. Stat. § 657.072(1)(b), was construed so as to include ministers of both churches and other religious organizations in Oregon's unemployment compensation program.The appellate court held that its construction of the statute did not violate the Free Exercise Clause of the United States Constitution, as the burden upon the church would be small compared with the State's overriding interest to provide for the economic well being of its citizens. The court did hold however, that the State was not obligated to pay interest on the unemployment compensation benefits, as there was nothing that indicated that the legislature intended that the State to pay interest on unemployment compensation benefits.


OUTCOME: The award of unemployment compensation benefits was affirmed, but the award of interest on those benefits was reversed.

 

151 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Macnab


SC S48039


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


334 Ore. 469; 51 P.3d 1249; 2002 Ore. LEXIS 584


March 1, 2002, Argued and Submitted  
August 15, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. MacNab, 2008 Ore. App. LEXIS 1292 (Or. Ct. App., Sept. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC CM9721537; CA A103792;.
State v. Macnab, 170 Ore. App. 538, 13 P.3d 167, 2000 Ore. App. LEXIS 1723 (2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in 1990 of first-degree sexual abuse. In 1997, defendant was charged with failing to register as a sex offender, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 181.599 (1995). Defendant claimed the statute was unconstitutional in that it violated the ex post facto clauses of the federal and state constitutions. Defendant was convicted, and the Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that, because Oregon did not have a sex offender registration law when he committed sexual abuse in 1987, requiring him to register as a sex offender under the 1995 law was an unconstitutional ex post facto application of law. The appellate court rejected defendant's arguments. The registration requirement did not subject defendant to undue restraint in the form of comprehensive or intrusive police scrutiny, control, or monitoring. Similarly, to the extent that defendant was deterred by the registration requirement from committing future crimes, the deterrent effect was a secondary or ancillary one, similar to the deterrent effect associated with civil sanctions such as driver license suspensions, bar suspensions, and disbarments. The registration requirement was regulatory, and no proof was presented that the registration scheme was punitive either in purpose or effect. Thus, because requiring defendant to register as a sex offender did not impose increased punishment not annexed to defendant's 1987 sex crime, it did not violate the ex post facto clause of the state or federal constitution.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals and the judgment of the trial court were affirmed.

Jensen v. Whitlow


SC S48130


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


334 Ore. 412; 51 P.3d 599; 2002 Ore. LEXIS 523


September 11, 2001, Argued and Submitted  
August 8, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] USDC CV99-1679 BR. On certified questions from the United States District Court for the District of Oregon dated December 28, 2000; certification accepted January 30, 2001. Michael R. Hogan, Chief Judge.



DISPOSITION: Certified questions answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff guardian filed a complaint in the United States District Court for the District of Oregon alleging that a foster parent abused her daughter while she was in the custody of Children Services Division of the State of Oregon (CSD), and that defendants, individual agents and employees of CSD, were negligent. The instant court accepted certified questions from the district court concerning the limitation of causes of action for such a tort.


OVERVIEW: The questions certified for review concerned whether Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.265(1) which limited a cause of action for any tort of a public employee to an action against the public body only and capped damages violated the terms of Or. Const. art. I, §§ 10, 17, or 20. The appellate court held that because the damages "cap" was not implicated in every case and did not render the remedy available to plaintiff incapable of restoring the right that has been injured. Thus, Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.265(1), on its face, did not violate the remedy clause under Or. Const. art. I, § 10. Further, the legislature did not violate Oregon's remedy guarantee by requiring substitution of the State as the only defendant in the action. The guardian had no "civil action" against the individual defendants. Thus, there was no claim to which a right to a jury trial could attach and § 30.265(1), on its face, did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 17. Finally, the legislature could reasonably conclude that providing public workers with personal immunity from liability for torts would assist public bodies in recruiting qualified persons. Accordingly, § 30.265(1), on its face, did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 20.


OUTCOME: In it's answers to the district court's certified questions, the appellate court held the statute which abolished any tort claim against individual officers, employees, or agents of a public body and substituted the public body as the sole defendant, did not violate the Oregon Constitution with regard to the right to a remedy, the right to a jury trial, or the privileges and immunities clause.

McLean v. Buck Med. Servs.

SUPREME COURT OF OREGON

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sued defendant employer, alleging violations of Or. Rev. The Oregon Appellate Court affirmed the summary judgment, and the employee sought further review.

Shilo Inn Portland/205 v. Multnomah County


SC S46816


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


333 Ore. 101; 36 P.3d 954; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 998


May 15, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
December 20, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * OTC 4370. Carl N. Byers, Judge.


*  Shilo Inn Portland/205, LLC. v. Multnomah County, 15 Ore. Tax 36, 1999 Ore. Tax LEXIS 17 (1999) 



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hotel filed a complaint and asserted that the amount it was required to pay in ad valorem property taxes was unconstitutional. The Oregon Tax Court granted the respondent urban renewal agency's motion for summary judgment. The hotel appealed.


OVERVIEW: The hotel owned two properties in Portland, within Multnomah County. Taxes that were assessed against the hotel were disbursed to the urban renewal agency. The hotel argued that part of the taxes that were designated on the tax bill for schools was actually paid to the urban renewal agency in excess of constitutionally established limits. The Tax Court held that taxes assessed on property located within an urban renewal area and used to pay urban renewal indebtedness did not have to be characterized as taxes raised to fund government operations other than the public school system. The state supreme court held that as Or. Const. art. XI, §11b established the rate that taxes could be imposed on property for government operations other than schools, the urban agency could not receive an amount greater than that rate. Or. Stat. § 310.150(7) did not require the taxing district to determine the appropriate category for an item of tax be based on the purpose to which that item of tax was dedicated, the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The grant of summary judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Hartung v. Bradbury


SC S48792, S48827, S48829, S48833, S48859, S48862, S48867, S48868, S48869, S48870, S48871, S48874, S48875 (Consolidated for Argument and Opinion)

332 Ore. 570; 33 P.3d 972; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 801


October 3, 2001, Argued and Submitted  
October 18, 2001, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petioners' motions were granted, dismissed and denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In original proceedings under Or. Const. art. IV, § 6(3), petitioners challenged the validity of a plan of reapportionment of legislative districts prepared by respondent Oregon Secretary of State. The petitions were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: One set of petitioners alleged that the Secretary of State violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 188.010(1) because he allegedly ignored geographic or political boundaries, communities of common interest, or transportation links. Another set of petitioners argued that that the official census data incorrectly attributed the population of one census block to another census block in another district. Another set of petitioners contended that the Secretary of State drew districts for the purpose of benefitting one political party in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 188.010(2). Another set of petitioners alleged that the Secretary of State improperly diluted the vote of Native Americans. The court granted only the petition of the set of petitioners who asserted the census data error, holding that the Secretary of State incorrectly assumed that, in determining the population of a district in the face of an admitted error in the census data, he nonetheless had to rely solely on official census data. The decision not to attempt to obtain additional or different reliable data regarding the population of a federal prison census block was one that no reasonable Secretary of State would have made.

Pennzoil Co. v. Dep't of Revenue


SC S47561


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


332 Ore. 542; 33 P.3d 314; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 702


March 2, 2001, Argued and Submitted  
October 4, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied March 18, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 1552.


PRIOR HISTORY: OTC 4301. On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * 


*  15 OTR 101 (2000).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Tax Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporate taxpayer appealed a judgment of the Oregon Tax Court, which determined that proceeds the taxpayer received in settlement of a tort judgment were apportionable business income.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer argued that: (1) the tax court erred in determining that the proceeds in question were business income; (2) the Due Process and Commerce Clauses of the United States Constitution prohibited Oregon from taxing the proceeds; and (3) the tax court erred by apportioning the proceeds under Or. Rev. Stat. § 314.670 (1987) in a manner that grossly and unconstitutionally distorted the taxpayer's activity in Oregon. Respondent Department of Revenue contended that an agreement between the taxpayer and another corporation for the taxpayer to purchase 3/7 of the corporation's stock gave rise to the proceeds. The instant court concluded that the proceeds were business income under the transactional test. Steps taken by the taxpayer to acquire an interest in established oil reserves were steps taken in the regular course of the taxpayer's trade or business. The fact that the taxpayer sought and received damages from a tortfeasor based on the cost of developing one billion barrels of oil reserves supported that conclusion. Because the disputed income arose from activity that served an operational function, Oregon could apportion that income.


OUTCOME: The tax court's judgment was affirmed.


 

160 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Branstetter


SC S47567


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


332 Ore. 389; 29 P.3d 1121; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 662


May 9, 2001, Argued and Submitted  
August 16, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended October 1, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC CR97-0077; CA A100029.


*  Appeal from Umatilla County Circuit Court, Rudy M. Murgo, Judge. 166 Ore. App. 286, 1 P.3d 451 (2000).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for consideration of the remaining assignments of error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with 12 counts of animal neglect, and petitioner state requested, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.347(1), forfeiture of defendant's animals. The trial court found the animals neglected and ordered them forfeited. Defendant was subsequently acquitted. The acquittals did not effect the forfeiture order. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) held that it did not have jurisdiction and dismissed the appeal. The defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals dismissed the defendant's appeal on the grounds that Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.053(1) was inapplicable, because it did not provide for an appeal from an acquittal, and therefore the court of appeals did not have jurisdiction to hear the appeal. On appeal, the defendant argued that the forfeiture order arose out of a "special statutory proceeding" and was therefore appealable under Or. Rev. Stat. § 19.205(4). The court reversed and remanded. Although the trial court's forfeiture order was issued during, and had the same case number as, defendant's prosecution on charges of animal neglect, the forfeiture order was based on a different statute, which was did not make the forfeiture contingent on the defendant being found guilty in the criminal case. The court held that despite a shared case name and number, the forfeiture proceeding was sufficiently separate and distinct from the criminal action to qualify as a special statutory proceeding. As a result, because the forfeiture order arose out of a special statutory proceeding, it was appealable under Or. Rev. Stat. § 19.205(4).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and the case was remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of the remaining assignments of error.

Sizemore v. Myers

August 9, 2001, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Ballot title referred to Attorney General for modification.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner electors sought review of a ballot title submitted by the attorney general (Oregon) concerning the use of public funds spent to collect or assist in the collection of money used for a political purpose.


OVERVIEW: Two ballot title review proceedings were consolidated. The fact that the court had certified different ballot titles for similarly worded measures did not preclude it from determining that the present ballot title substantially complied with Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035. The challenge to the summary of the certified ballot title, however, was well taken. The summary misstated existing law and failed to comply substantially with Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(2)(d). The summary connoted that current law restricted or reduced, but did not prohibit, public employees from collecting money for a political purpose on work time. That was not an accurate statement of current law. Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.432(2) already prohibited public employees from collecting or attempting to collect political contributions during work hours.


OUTCOME: The ballot title was referred to the attorney general for modification of the summary.

Hyundai Semiconductor Am. v. City of Eugene


SC S46528


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


332 Ore. 293; 27 P.3d 124; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 537


September 11, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
July 6, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * OTC 4167. Carl N. Byers, Judge.


*  14 OTR 557 (1999).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Tax Court is vacated. The case is remanded to the Tax Court with instructions to dismiss the complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a tax case, the Oregon Tax Court ruled that a condition imposed by defendant city and defendant county on plaintiff corporation for a property tax exemption in an enterprise zone violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 285.577(4) (1995). The city and county sought review.


OVERVIEW: In 1986, the city and county cosponsored the establishment of an enterprise zone, but in 1997 the city and county decided not to seek renewal of the zone. The city and county also decided to impose an additional condition before the zone terminated. Qualified businesses were required to make a public benefit contribution of up to 15 percent of the tax exemption in order to qualify for the exemption. The corporation filed a complaint in the tax court, seeking, among other things, a declaration that the public benefit contribution requirement violated § 285.577(4). The city and county argued that the corporation's claims were not ripe and that the corporation had failed to exhaust administrative remedies. The instant court held that: (1) the case was ripe; (2) Or. Rev. Stat. § 305.275(4) (1995) required the corporation to exhaust its administrative remedies before filing its complaint in the tax court, but the corporation failed to do so; (3) the tax court had no discretion to reach the merits of the corporation's claim because of the corporation's failure to comply with § 305.275(4); and (4) the tax court should have dismissed the case.


OUTCOME: The tax court's judgment was vacated and the case was remanded to the tax court with instructions to dismiss the corporation's complaint.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed, without opinion, defendant's convictions for first degree burglary, Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.225, and first degree aggravated theft, Or. Rev. Stat. § 164.057. Defendant petitioned for review.

Rev. Stat. § 136.005. The instant court also declined to address defendant's claim that removal of the juror in question offended his "structural" right to a fair trial.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court and the judgment of the trial court were affirmed.

State v. Fugate


SC S45976


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


332 Ore. 195; 26 P.3d 802; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 369


October 14, 1999, Argued and Submitted  
June 8, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended August 14, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: CC 96-0660-C; CA A96628. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


*  Appeal from Jackson County District Court, Rebecca G. Orf, Judge. 154 Or App 643, 963 P.2d 686, modified and adhered to on recons 156 Or App 609, 969 P.2d 395 (1998).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed, and the case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving while under the influence (DUI) in the Jackson County District Court (Oregon). Defendant filed a pretrial motion to suppress any evidence of his impairment due to alcohol. The trial court granted the motion. The Oregon Court of Appeals reversed, and defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the arresting officer exceeded the permissible scope of the traffic stop that preceded defendant's arrest. The court of appeals held that 1997 Or. Laws ch. 313 (S.B. 936 (Or. 1996)), enacted after the trial court's decision, applied to the case and allowed admission of the evidence. S.B. 936 expanded the admissibility of relevant evidence in a criminal action. The supreme court reversed, holding first that S.B. 936 embraced only a single subject and matters properly connected therewith, and did not exceed the scope of its title, thereby complying with Or. Const. art. IV, § 20. However, the part of the law on which the State relied could not be applied retroactively to defendant's case. The provisions affecting the rules of evidence violated the restriction against ex post facto laws under Or. Const. art. I, § 21. The legislature clearly intended that the act apply retrospectively, depriving a criminal defendant of a right to have evidence suppressed under certain conditions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of the State's remaining arguments concerning admissibility of the evidence.

Smothers v. Gresham Transfer, Inc.


SC S44512


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


332 Ore. 83; 23 P.3d 333; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 298


November 8, 1999, Argued and Submitted  
May 10, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 9505-02969; CA A90805. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Henry Kantor, Judge. 149 Ore. App. 49, 941 P.2d 1065 (1997).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee filed a claim under Oregon's Workers' Compensation Law, Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.001 et seq. Petitioner's employer's insurer denied the claim, and an administrative law judge (ALJ) upheld that decision. Petitioner then filed a negligence action seeking compensation for work-related injuries. The Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon) dismissed the action, and the Court of Appeals of Oregon affirmed. Petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Employee worked for a trucking company and was exposed to chemicals other employees used to clean the company's trucks. Employee developed an upper respiratory infection, pneumonia, and bronchitis, and he filed a claim for workers' compensation benefits. Trucking company's insurer rejected the claim and an ALJ upheld that decision because employee failed to prove that his exposure to chemicals at work was the major contributing cause of his disorder. After his claim was denied, employee filed a negligence action against his employer, but the trial court dismissed that action, reasoning that Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.018 (1995) made Oregon's Workers' Compensation Law the exclusive remedy for work-related injuries. The state supreme court held that the § 656.018 violated the remedies clause of the Oregon Constitution because, by making the Workers' Compensation Law the exclusive remedy for work-related injuries and imposing a requirement on employee to establish that injuries sustained at work were the major contributing cause of his condition , § 656.018 abrogated employee's common-law right to sue employer for negligence.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the decision of the intermediate appellate court, and the judgment of the trial court, and it remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Crocker & Crocker


SC S46166


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


332 Ore. 42; 22 P.3d 759; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 273


November 8, 1999, Argued and Submitted; December 19, 2000, Reassigned  
April 26, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 8706-64201; CA A99888. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


*  Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Paula J. Kurshner, Judge. 157 Ore. App. 651, 971 P.2d 469 (1998).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The order of the circuit court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversing the trial court's order which dismissed respondent's motion to modify petitioner's child support obligation in order to provide support for the parties' 18-year-old child who was planning to attend college.


OVERVIEW: Respondent mother moved to modify petitioner father's child support obligation to provide support for the parties' 18-year-old child who was planning to attend college. Petitioner opposed the motion, arguing that the statute that permitted such an order, Or. Rev. Stat. § 107.108(1), violated Or. Const. art. I, § 20 and the Equal Protection Clause. The trial court dismissed respondent's motion, and the appellate court reversed the trial court. On further review, the supreme court affirmed the appellate court and reversed the trial court. Section 107.108(1) provided for disparate treatment based upon one's marital status. Accordingly, the legislative classification was based upon personal characteristics that were not immutable, such that the classification survived scrutiny under art. I, § 20 if the legislature had a rational basis for making the classification. Such a rational basis existed. The same constitutional analysis applied under the Equal Protection Clause in this case.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's decision was affirmed and the trial court's order was reversed. The relevant legislative classification which provided for disparate treatment based upon one's marital status survived scrutiny under state equal-privileges constitutional doctrine, since the legislature had a rational basis for making the classification.

Outdoor Media Dimensions v. State


SC S44590


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


331 Ore. 634; 20 P.3d 180; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 135


November 9, 1998, Argued and Submitted  
March 8, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 94-3953-L2; CA A91779. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Appeal from Jackson County Circuit Court, Loren L. Sawyer, Judge. 326 Ore. 627, 150 Ore. App. 106, 945 P.2d 614 (1997).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Through claims under conversion and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, plaintiff advertising company, challenged the Oregon Motorist Information Act (OMIA), Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 377.700 to 377.840, 377.992, claiming, inter alia, it violated its free speech and due process rights. The trial court granted summary judgment to defendants, the State and three employees. The Oregon Court of Appeals affirmed. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: The company had three billboards, none of which were in compliance with the OMIA. The State removed one billboard from which the company had removed all language, and the company requested administrative hearings as to the others. After losing at one hearing, the company removed language from the second sign. The summary judgment for the State was correct. The pleadings did not indicate that the claim for conversion rested on the alleged unconstitutionality of OMIA, and the issue on appeal was inadequately briefed. Under OMIA, rendering the sign blank did not constitute compliance, and the State could remove the noncomplying sign without waiting six months. The company's estoppel argument, based on alleged statements by a State employee, was also not adequately developed. The claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 failed because the company did not plead or argued that the state's application of OMIA caused it money damages. Because law regarding the right to free speech as affected by billboard regulation was not clearly established, the State was entitled to summary judgment based upon qualified immunity. The company did not exhaust its administrative remedies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate decision and the circuit court's judgment.

Parrott v. Carr Chevrolet, Inc.


SC S45916, S45917 (Cases Consolidated For Argument And Decision)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


331 Ore. 537; 17 P.3d 473; 2001 Ore. LEXIS 1


September 8, 1999, Argued and Submitted  
January 11, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Parrott v. Carr Chevrolet, Inc., 2001 Ore. LEXIS 223 (Or., Mar. 27, 2001)
Related proceeding at Carr Chevrolet, Inc. v. Am. Hardware Mut. Ins. Co., 2003 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 25631 (D. Or., May 19, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC C93-0873CV; CA A88512. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


*  Appeal from Washington County Circuit Court, Michael J. McElligott, Judge. 156 Ore. App. 257, 965 P.2d 440 (1998).


Parrott v. Carr Chevrolet, Inc., 156 Or. App. 257, 965 P.2d 440, 1998 Ore. App. LEXIS 1560 (1998)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court with instructions to reinstate the jury's $ 1 million punitive damages award.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action brought by plaintiff against defendant in which plaintiff alleged defendant violated the Unlawful Trade Practices Act, both parties appealed a judgment of the Oregon Court of Appeals reversing trial court's reduction of $ 1 million punitive damages award and remanding with instructions to enter judgment allowing defendant's motion for new trial unless plaintiff filed a remittitur of punitive damages in the amount of $ 300,000.


OVERVIEW: This case arose from defendant's sale of a used motor vehicle to plaintiff. The jury returned a verdict for plaintiff and awarded $ 11,496 in compensatory damages and $ 1 million in punitive damages. The trial court reduced the punitive damages award to $ 50,000, but the appellate court directed the trial court to grant defendant's motion for new trial unless plaintiff filed a remittitur of punitive damages in the amount of $ 300,000. Defendant challenged the jury's $ 1 million punitive damages award as excessive. The primary issue on review was the appropriate standard for post-verdict judicial review of a punitive damages award. The Oregon Supreme Court held that the jury's $ 1 million award of punitive damages was within the range that a rationale juror would have been entitled to award; it concluded that plaintiff established that defendant's misconduct was part of its day-to-day business dealings and was not limited to the sale of the vehicle to plaintiff and that defendant's conduct was highly reprehensible. The jury's punitive damages award was not grossly excessive in violation of the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case was remanded to the trial court with instructions to reinstate jury's $ 1 million punitive damages award. Jury's punitive damages award was not grossly excessive, as the award was within the range that a rationale juror would have been entitled to award in light of the record as a whole.

State v. Fleetwood


SC S41311


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


331 Ore. 511; 16 P.3d 503; 2000 Ore. LEXIS 986


March 9, 1995, Resubmitted; June 11, 1998, Argued and Submitted  
December 29, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC CR92167; CA A77709. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


* Appeal from Wasco County Circuit Court. John V. Kelly, Judge. 127 Ore. App. 558, 872 P.2d 998 (1994).

.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals reversed. The order of the circuit court affirmed, and the case remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) decision reversing the trial court decision granting his motion to suppress statement made to an informant and recorded with the use of an electronic listening device, claiming the statements were unconstitutionally obtained.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with distribution of a controlled substance based on comments made while an informant, who was wearing a listening device, was present. He moved to suppress the evidence. The trial court granted the motion, but the court of appeals reversed. However, the Oregon Supreme Court reversed. Comments made by defendant during a telephone call from his home, while the informant was present, were a wire communication pursuant to Ore. Rev. Stat. § 165.535(4). Neither the informant or the listening officer was a participant in defendant's telecommunication. Neither defendant or the other participant in the call consented to the state's obtaining defendant's statements during the call by the body wire device. Defendant's conversations with his mother in the home and a juvenile outside the home were also protected. The state did not satisfy Ore. Rev. Stat. § 165.540(5)(a)(B) because the listening officer did not share reciprocally in the communication as a participant jointly engaged in conversation. Defendant's conversations with the informant were suppressed because the state did not obtain a court order.


OUTCOME: Court of Appeals judgment reversed and cause remanded because the state did not comply with the statutory requirements governing electronic listening devices.

State v. Lotches


SC S40460


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


331 Ore. 455; 17 P.3d 1045; 2000 Ore. LEXIS 987


March 6, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
December 29, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied October 1, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 5768.
Writ of certiorari denied Lotches v. Oregon, 534 U.S. 833, 122 S. Ct. 82, 151 L. Ed. 2d 45, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 5768 (2001)
Post-conviction relief denied at Lotches v. Premo, 2013 Ore. App. LEXIS 831 (Or. Ct. App., July 10, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 92-08-34431. On automatic and direct review of the judgments of conviction and the sentence of death imposed by the Multnomah County Circuit Court. William Snouffer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of conviction for aggravated murder on counts 1 and 2 are reversed. The judgment of conviction for aggravated murder on count 3 and the sentence of death are affirmed. The case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings with respect to counts 1 and 2.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant received automatic and direct review of his convictions for three counts of aggravated murder, one count of attempted aggravated murder, one count of attempted murder, one count of assault in the first degree with a firearm, one count of robbery in the first degree, and one count of felon in possession of a firearm, and sentence of death imposed by the Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon).


OVERVIEW: An altercation between defendant and an acquaintance rapidly escalated into a melee that included an attempted car-jacking, two shoot-outs between defendant and security officers, and resulted in the death of one officer, defendant's conviction for three counts of aggravated murder, and a sentence of death. On automatic appeal, the court reversed on two of the murder counts and otherwise affirmed. The trial court's aggravated murder instructions concerning robbery and kidnapping and killing to conceal identity were erroneous because they neither limited the jury's consideration to a specified underlying felony nor required jury unanimity concerning a choice among alternative felonies, as Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095 required. Neither the sufficiency of the evidence nor the prosecutor's closing argument were a legally sufficient substitute for the necessary instructions. As the jury's unanimous conviction of defendant for one attempted murder provided the predicate for his conviction related to concealing identity, that jury instruction error was harmless. Juror selection had been proper.


OUTCOME: Aggravated murder convictions on two counts were reversed, the case remanded, and all else was affirmed. Instructions that neither limited jury consideration to a specified underlying felony to aggravated murder counts, nor required unanimity concerning a choice among alternative felonies, were erroneous and cured by neither evidence nor closing arguments. That error was harmless in regard to one count. Defendant waived his right to testify.

State v. Amini


SC S45699


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


331 Ore. 384; 15 P.3d 541; 2000 Ore. LEXIS 938


November 10, 1999, Argued and Submitted  
December 14, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC 94-01-30513; CA A88710.


*  Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Stephen S. Walker, Judge. 154 Ore. App. 589, 963 P.2d 65 (1998).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State of Oregon sought review of Oregon court of appeals ruling that defendant was entitled to a new trial because a jury instruction that advised jurors of the consequences of a guilty except for insanity verdict violated defendant's rights to a fair trial under Or. Const. art. I, amend. 11.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder and related crimes, and raised the affirmative defense of insanity. The trial court gave Or. Unif. Jury Instruction - Crim. 1122, which advised jurors that if they found defendant guilty except for insanity that defendant would have been placed in the custody of the Psychiatric Security Review Board, and the conditions on which defendant could have been released. The jury was instructed that it was not to consider what sentence the court might have imposed if defendant was found guilty. Defendant was convicted and appealed; the court of appeals reversed, ruling that the instruction violated defendant's right to a fair trial under Or. Const. art. I, § 11. The court reversed, holding that the Or. Const. art. I, § 11 guarantee of the right to trial by an impartial jury meant trial in an unbiased venue, not a trial that was fair in all respects. The instruction had no tendency to deprive defendant of trial by an impartial jury. However, U.S. Const. amends. VI, XIV did guarantee defendant a fair trial, and the case was remanded for consideration of whether the instruction violated those federal constitutional provisions.


OUTCOME: Decision reversed, because guarantee of an impartial jury did not equate to right to a fair trial that would bar use of the challenged jury instruction. The case was remanded with orders that the court of appeals determine whether the instruction violated respondent's rights.

330 Ore. 517; 8 P.3d 966; 2000 Ore. LEXIS 647

DISPOSITION: The accused is reprimanded.

OUTCOME: Court held that petitioner was not estopped from prosecuting respondent and ordered that respondent be reprimanded. State v. McNeely


SC S41941


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


330 Ore. 457; 8 P.3d 212; 2000 Ore. LEXIS 615


March 8, 2000, Argued and Submitted  
August 3, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied December 11, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 8377.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On automatic and direct review of the judgment of conviction and sentence of death imposed by the Multnomah County Circuit Court. CC 93-05-33276. Ellen Rosenblum, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction and sentence of death are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On automatic and direct review, defendant challenged his criminal conviction for aggravated murder and sentence of death imposed by the Multnomah County Circuit Court (Oregon).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated murder and sentenced to death. One state witness was an inmate who gained information from defendant while incarcerated. Because the inmate gathered the information and provided it to police without direction from the state, the inmate was not an agent of the state, and the information was admissible. The inmate's failure to identify defendant at trial was not cause to suppress his testimony, but rather went to the jury's determination of the inmate's credibility as a witness. The prosecution's reference to the victim during closing argument did not deny defendant his right to confront witnesses. Prosecution's statements during the penalty phase of the trial regarding defendant's history of failure to take responsibility for his actions was relevant to the propensity for future dangerousness of defendant and did not constitute a comment regarding defendant's failure to testify at trial. Prosecution was permitted to present argument in support of a death sentence. Therefore, defendant's conviction and sentence of death were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction for aggravated murder and defendant's sentence of death were affirmed, where the court did not err in admitting the testimony of an inmate regarding defendant's statements during confinement. The court properly denied defendant's motion for a mistrial based upon prosecution's reference to the victim, and properly allowed the prosecution to argue in support of the death sentence.

177 of 770 DOCUMENTS


Warrenton Fiber Co. v. DOE


A155371


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


283 Ore. App. 270; 388 P.3d 372; 2016 Ore. App. LEXIS 1624


August 13, 2015, Argued and Submitted 
December 29, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 122419. Clatsop County Circuit Court. Philip L. Nelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded on appeal; cross-appeal dismissed as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The department properly determined that the business was not eligible for a biomass tax credit for tree bark that it stripped from pulp logs at its mill and then sold as hog fuel because the rule relied upon by the department was valid, when the legislature intended the biomass category of "forest or rangeland woody debris from harvesting or thinning" to mean organic material that was debris when collected from the forest or rangeland, and that definition was consistent with Or. Admin. R. 330-170-0040(1)(c), which categorized "residual wood waste from mill operations" as not biomass.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and case remanded on appeal, and cross-appeal dismissed.

 

178 of 770 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dickas


A156174


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


283 Ore. App. 59; 388 P.3d 1121; 2016 Ore. App. LEXIS 1610


May 27, 2015, Argued and Submitted 
December 21, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Multnomah County Circuit Court. PR00553244. Karin Johana Immergut, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant was found guilty of violating the posting speed limit under Or. Rev. Stat. § 811.111, the charge having been made in a traffic citation based on a photo-radar operation in a highway work zone, and the court affirmed; [2]-The photo-radar unit was operated by city police officers in a work zone on a state highway pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 810.441, and therefore the citation was issued under the authority of Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 810.441, 810.442; [3]-Those statutes do not apply to only limited parts of the state, and together they appear to allow for the operation of photo radar by the Oregon Department of Transportation or its agents and for citations based on that photo radar in highway work zones on state highways throughout the state; [4]-Defendant's arguments focused on different statutes that did not apply, and he presented no cogent argument for reversal.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.
